Why Knowing the Definition of Syllogism is Not Enough
Author argues for the necessity of learning how to acquire syllogistic premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why knowledge of a syllogism's definition is insufficient for constructing valid syllogisms, using the medicine analogy from the text.

Core Idea
Knowing what a syllogism is doesn't give you one.

One of the most common errors in logic is thinking that understanding the definition of a syllogism is enough to construct one. The author of our passage uses a powerful medical analogy to show otherwise. Just as knowing what a medicine is does not cure you unless you know how to obtain and administer it, knowing the definition of a syllogism does not help you reason correctly unless you know how to acquire its premises. This lesson will help you internalize why the method of acquisition is as important as the definition itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Analogy
The author compares knowledge of syllogism to knowledge of medicine. What is the specific parallel? Write it out.
2
Extract the Key Claim
What does the author say is the consequence of only knowing the definition? He says it would not suffice for curing the disease or for obtaining the syllogism.
Tie this to your own study of logic.
3
Generalize the Principle
The principle is that practical know-how (how to acquire premises) is distinct from conceptual knowledge (what the thing is). Can you think of another Islamic science where this distinction applies?
Consider fiqh: knowing the ruling is not enough; you need to know how to derive it.
4
Apply to Your Own Learning
Why does the author say that learning acquisition in a universal way is necessary? Because we study syllogism as syllogism, not just one type.
This universal approach ensures you can handle any syllogistic argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام في اكتساب المقدمات وتحصيل القياسات فذلك من اللوازم لأنه لا يمكننا أن نميز القياس الصحيح من غير الصحيح كما ينبغي. وإن الصحيح منه ما هو وكيف هو وكم هو ؟ وما الفرق بين البسيط منه والمركب؟ إلا وأن نعلم كيفية اكتسابه وتحصيله فإنا إذا احتجنا إلى معرفة شيء بالقياس لم يكفنا العلم بأن القياس ما هو كما أن علمنا بأن الدواء النافع لعلة كذا ما هو لم يكفنا شفاء تلك العلة إلا وأن نعلم وجه طلبه وتحصيله وإيجاده. فإنه إن اتفق أن يصادفه معمولا محصلا يمكننا الانتفاع به وإلا لا يمكن أصلا ولا ينفعنــا العلم بماهيته. ولو كان كذلك لكان العلم بكيفية اكتساب القياس وتحصيله من اللوازم فكان الاشتغال بتعرف اكتسابه على وجه كلي يعم القياس البرهاني والجدلي وغيرهما من اللوازم أيضًا لما أن بحثنا في القياس من حيث هو القياس لا في القياس الذي هو كذا أو كذا فنقول: إن الأمور إما شخصيات وإما كليات والشخصيات قد تكون موجودة بالحقيقة في الشخصيات كهذا البياض في هذا الشيء مثلا، ولا تكون بالحقيقة عليها كهذا الأبيض على هذا الشيء؛ فإن ذلك ليس حملا حقيقيا وقد اتضح لك هذا من قبل. وأما الكليات فإنها هي المحمولات بالحقيقة وهي إما قريبة من الأشخاص وإما بعيدة وإما متوسطة، وستعرفها في كتاب البرهان أنها كما هي متناهية في جانب التنازل إلى الأشخاص التي لا تحمل على غيرها بالحقيقة فكذلك في جانب التصاعد إلى محمولات لا يحمل عليها غيرها أصلا. ولما كانت متناهية من الطرفين فقد كانت الأعداد الواقعة بينهما متوسطة وإلا لكانت اللا متناهية محصورة بين حاضرين. ثم إذا أردت أن تكتسب القياس فضع الحدين أعني الأصغر والأكبر، واطلب حد كل واحد منهما وكل ما يمكن حمله على كل واحد منهما من الأنواع والأجناس والفصول والخواص والعوارض وما يحمل على كل واحد من هذه الخمسة من الأنواع والأجناس والفصول والخواص وأعراضها. وقد علمت من قبل أن لكل واحد منها أنواعًا وأجناسا وفصولا وخواص وأعراضًا وهي لواحق اللواحق. وعلى هذا مرة بعد أخرى إلى أن قدرت. فإن جميع ذلك محمول على الحد ثم يطلب جميع ما يلحقه كل واحد من الحدين المذكورين بالنسبة التي مرَّ ذكرها وهي الأمور التي يكون الحد نوعًا لها أو جنسًا أو فصلا أو خاصة أو عرضًا عاماً كذلك. وكذلك بهذه النسبة مرة بعد أخرى إلى أن قدرت؛ فهذه مواد طلب الإيجاب. وأما مواد السلب فاطلب أيضًا جميع الأمور التي لا يوجد لحد حد منها ضرورة أو إطلاقا. ولا تشتغل بطلب ما لا يلحقه حد حد، فإن الذي لا يلحقه حد حد هو نفس ما
And as for the discourse on acquiring premises and obtaining syllogisms, that is from the necessities because we cannot distinguish the correct syllogism from the incorrect as we should. And [we cannot know] what is the correct one, how it is, and how many it is, and what is the difference between the simple and the compound, unless we know the manner of its acquisition and attainment. For if we need to know something by syllogism, the knowledge of what the syllogism is does not suffice us, just as our knowledge of what the beneficial medicine for a certain illness is does not suffice us for the cure of that illness unless we know the way to seek it, obtain it, and find it. For if it happens that one encounters it already prepared and obtained, we can benefit from it, otherwise it is absolutely impossible and the knowledge of its essence does not benefit us. And if it were otherwise, then the knowledge of how to acquire the syllogism and obtain it would be from the necessities, so occupying oneself with learning its acquisition in a universal way that encompasses the demonstrative, dialectical, and other syllogisms is also from the necessities, because our investigation is about the syllogism inasmuch as it is a syllogism, not about the syllogism that is such and such. So we say: Things are either particulars or universals. And particulars may be really existent in the particulars, like this whiteness in this thing for example, and may not be really predicated of them, like this white thing upon this thing; for that is not a real predication, and this has become clear to you before. As for universals, they are the real predicates, and they are either near to the particulars, or far, or intermediate, and you will know them in the Book of Demonstration that just as they are finite in the descending direction to the particulars which are not predicated of anything else truly, so too in the ascending direction to predicates which are not predicated upon anything else at all. And since they are finite from both ends, the numbers occurring between them are intermediate, otherwise the infinite would be bounded by two [limits]. Then, when you want to acquire the syllogism, posit the two limits, i.e., the minor and the major, and seek the definition of each one of them and everything that can be predicated of each one of them from the species, genera, differentiae, properties, and accidents, and what is predicated of each one of these five from species, genera, differentiae, properties, and their accidents. And you have already known before that each one of them has species, genera, differentiae, properties, and accidents, and they are the accidents of accidents. And upon this again and again until you have the ability. For all of that is predicated of the limit, then seek everything that attaches to each one of the two mentioned limits with respect to the relationship that was mentioned earlier, i.e., the things of which the limit is a species or a genus or a differentia or a property or a common accident, similarly. And also with this relationship again and again until you have the ability; these are the materials for seeking affirmation. As for the materials for negation, also seek all the things that do not exist for each limit, necessarily or absolutely. And do not busy yourself with seeking what does not attach to each limit, for what does not attach to each limit is the same as what...
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage explains the necessity of learning how to acquire premises for syllogisms. The author argues that knowing what a syllogism is does not suffice; one must know how to construct it, just as knowing what a medicine is does not cure a disease unless one knows how to obtain and administer it. The author then divides things into particulars and universals, noting that particulars can be really existent in subjects but not truly predicated of them (e.g., 'this whiteness in this thing' is real, but 'this white thing' is not a real predication). Universals are the real predicates and are arranged in a hierarchy: near, far, and intermediate. They are finite in both ascending and descending directions. The author then provides a method for constructing a syllogism: posit the minor and major terms, then seek their definitions and everything predicable of them (species, genera, differentiae, properties, accidents), and then also seek what is predicated of those five, recursively. This is repeated until one has exhausted the possibilities. This yields the materials for affirmative propositions. For negative propositions, one seeks what is not found for each term necessarily or absolutely. The author warns against seeking what does not attach to each term, as that would be wasted effort. The passage provides a systematic procedure for discovering middle terms.

الكليات
الحد الأصغر
الحد الأكبر

According to the passage, why is knowing the definition of a syllogism insufficient?
A) Because definitions are always incomplete
B) Because one must also know how to acquire and construct the syllogism
C) Because syllogisms are not definable
D) Because the definition is too difficult to understand

Key Takeaway
Knowing the definition of a syllogism is necessary but not sufficient; you must also master the procedure for acquiring its premises.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Particulars vs. Universals: The Building Blocks of Premises
Author classifies things into particulars and universals and explains the nature of real predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between particulars (shakhṣiyyāt) and universals (kulliyyāt) and identify whether a predicate is a real predication.

Core Idea
Universals are real predicates; particulars are not.

Before you can acquire premises for a syllogism, you must understand the nature of the terms you are working with. The passage divides all things into particulars and universals. Particulars are individual instances that cannot be truly predicated of others, while universals are real predicates that can be applied to many things. This lesson will help you recognize the difference, using examples from Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Definition of Particulars
The passage says 'particulars may be really existent in the particulars' like 'this whiteness in this thing'. Particulars are individual, non-predicable entities. Write down an example from your own knowledge.
2
Understand the Definition of Universals
Universals are 'real predicates' and are classified as near, far, and intermediate relative to particulars. Give an example of a universal that is near, one that is far.
Near: 'human' near to Zayd. Far: 'substance' far from Zayd.
3
Apply the Test: Real vs. Non-Real Predication
The author gives an example: 'this white thing' is not a real predication of 'this thing', but 'whiteness' is real. Why? Because universals are real predicates; particulars are not.
Think about the logical difference: a universal can be said of many, a particular cannot.
4
Identify the Hierarchy
Universals are finite in ascending and descending directions. Map out the hierarchy from the most particular to the most universal.
This hierarchy prepares you for finding middle terms in syllogisms.

The Syllogism
intermediate

The Method: How to Discover Premises for Your Syllogism
Author provides a step-by-step method for acquiring syllogistic premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the step-by-step procedure for discovering predicable terms for the minor and major terms of a syllogism, as described in the passage.

Core Idea
To build a syllogism, recursively find all predicates of your terms.

After understanding the necessity of acquisition and the distinction between particulars and universals, the author provides a concrete method. You start by positing the minor and major terms, then seek their definitions and everything predicable of them via the five predicables. You then repeat the process for those predicates. This systematic recursion yields the materials for affirmative and negative premises. This lesson will walk you through that procedure.

Guided Path
1
Posit the Two Limits (Minor and Major Terms)
The first step is to identify the subject and predicate of the conclusion you want to prove. For example, in 'Wine is forbidden', the minor term is 'wine', the major is 'forbidden'. Write down the minor and major for a syllogism you want to construct.
2
Find All Predicable Terms for Each Limit
Using the five predicables (species, genus, differentia, property, accident), list everything that can be truly predicated of the minor term and of the major term.
For 'wine', you might list: 'intoxicant' (species?), 'beverage' (genus), 'intoxicating' (differentia?), etc.
3
Extend the Search Recursively
For each of the predicables you found, also find what can be predicated of them (accidents of accidents). Repeat until you have a rich enough set of terms.
This ensures you don't miss a potential middle term.
4
Identify Materials for Affirmation and Negation
The passage distinguishes between materials for positive and negative propositions. For a middle term that connects, you need an affirmative relationship. Also, understand what is not predicable (materials for negation).
The goal is to find a middle term that is predicated of one limit and the other limit predicated of it, or vice versa.

واحد منها أنواعًا وأجناسا وفصولا وخواص وأعراضًا وهي لواحق اللواحق. وعلى هذا مرة بعد أخرى إلى أن قدرت. فإن جميع ذلك محمول على الحد ثم يطلب جميع ما يلحقه كل واحد من الحدين المذكورين بالنسبة التي مرَّ ذكرها وهي الأمور التي يكون الحد نوعًا لها أو جنسًا أو فصلا أو خاصة أو عرضًا عاماً كذلك. وكذلك بهذه النسبة مرة بعد أخرى إلى أن قدرت؛ فهذه مواد طلب الإيجاب. وأما مواد السلب فاطلب أيضًا جميع الأمور التي لا يوجد لحد حد منها ضرورة أو إطلاقا. ولا تشتغل بطلب ما لا يلحقه حد حد، فإن الذي لا يلحقه حد حد هو نفس ما
And as for the discourse on acquiring premises and obtaining syllogisms, that is from the necessities because we cannot distinguish the correct syllogism from the incorrect as we should. And [we cannot know] what is the correct one, how it is, and how many it is, and what is the difference between the simple and the compound, unless we know the manner of its acquisition and attainment. For if we need to know something by syllogism, the knowledge of what the syllogism is does not suffice us, just as our knowledge of what the beneficial medicine for a certain illness is does not suffice us for the cure of that illness unless we know the way to seek it, obtain it, and find it. For if it happens that one encounters it already prepared and obtained, we can benefit from it, otherwise it is absolutely impossible and the knowledge of its essence does not benefit us. And if it were otherwise, then the knowledge of how to acquire the syllogism and obtain it would be from the necessities, so occupying oneself with learning its acquisition in a universal way that encompasses the demonstrative, dialectical, and other syllogisms is also from the necessities, because our investigation is about the syllogism inasmuch as it is a syllogism, not about the syllogism that is such and such. So we say: Things are either particulars or universals. And particulars may be really existent in the particulars, like this whiteness in this thing for example, and may not be really predicated of them, like this white thing upon this thing; for that is not a real predication, and this has become clear to you before. As for universals, they are the real predicates, and they are either near to the particulars, or far, or intermediate, and you will know them in the Book of Demonstration that just as they are finite in the descending direction to the particulars which are not predicated of anything else truly, so too in the ascending direction to predicates which are not predicated upon anything else at all. And since they are finite from both ends, the numbers occurring between them are intermediate, otherwise the infinite would be bounded by two [limits]. Then, when you want to acquire the syllogism, posit the two limits, i.e., the minor and the major, and seek the definition of each one of them and everything that can be predicated of each one of them from the species, genera, differentiae, properties, and accidents, and what is predicated of each one of these five from species, genera, differentiae, properties, and their accidents. And you have already known before that each one of them has species, genera, differentiae, properties, and accidents, and they are the accidents of accidents. And upon this again and again until you have the ability. For all of that is predicated of the limit, then seek everything that attaches to each one of the two mentioned limits with respect to the relationship that was mentioned earlier, i.e., the things of which the limit is a species or a genus or a differentia or a property or a common accident, similarly. And also with this relationship again and again until you have the ability; these are the materials for seeking affirmation. As for the materials for negation, also seek all the things that do not exist for each limit, necessarily or absolutely. And do not busy yourself with seeking what does not attach to each limit, for what does not attach to each limit is the same as what...
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage explains the necessity of learning how to acquire premises for syllogisms. The author argues that knowing what a syllogism is does not suffice; one must know how to construct it, just as knowing what a medicine is does not cure a disease unless one knows how to obtain and administer it. The author then divides things into particulars and universals, noting that particulars can be really existent in subjects but not truly predicated of them (e.g., 'this whiteness in this thing' is real, but 'this white thing' is not a real predication). Universals are the real predicates and are arranged in a hierarchy: near, far, and intermediate. They are finite in both ascending and descending directions. The author then provides a method for constructing a syllogism: posit the minor and major terms, then seek their definitions and everything predicable of them (species, genera, differentiae, properties, accidents), and then also seek what is predicated of those five, recursively. This is repeated until one has exhausted the possibilities. This yields the materials for affirmative propositions. For negative propositions, one seeks what is not found for each term necessarily or absolutely. The author warns against seeking what does not attach to each term, as that would be wasted effort. The passage provides a systematic procedure for discovering middle terms.

The key to constructing a syllogism is a systematic, recursive search for all predicables of the minor and major terms, using the five predicables as a guide.

The Syllogism
intermediate
6 min
The Method of Seeking Predicates and Negatives for Syllogism Construction
Author explains the method to construct a syllogism by exhaustive search.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the step-by-step method of exhaustively searching for predicates and negatives of the terms to construct a syllogism.

Core Idea
A syllogism is built by systematically searching all predicates and negatives of the terms.

Building a syllogism is not merely finding any middle term; it requires a systematic search. The author provides a method: start with the two extremes (minor and major), then recursively find all predicates—genera, species, differentiae, properties, accidents—and also all negatives. This ensures you have the materials for a valid inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor and major terms
The minor term (subject) and major term (predicate) of the desired conclusion are the two extremes. Write them down as the starting point.
2
List all predicates of each term
For each extreme, list everything that can be predicated of it: its species, genera, differentiae, properties, accidents, and any other terms that can be truly affirmed. This is the 'materials of affirmation'.
The author calls these 'mawādd al-ījāb'.
3
List all negatives of each term
For each extreme, list everything that is necessarily or absolutely not predicated of it. This is the 'materials of negation' (mawādd al-salb). Do not waste time on irrelevant terms.
4
Recurse: list predicates of the predicates
Take the predicates you found for each extreme, and for each of those, list their own predicates (genera, species, etc.). Continue recursively 'again and again' to build a comprehensive stock of terms.
The author calls these 'concomitants of concomitants' (lawāḥiq al-lawāḥiq).
5
Use the collected terms to find a middle term
With the exhaustive lists of affirmative and negative materials, look for a term that appears in the affirmative list of the minor and also relates to the major (or vice versa). This term becomes the middle term for a valid syllogism.
The middle term must connect the extremes according to the conditions of the valid syllogistic figures.

ثم إذا أردت أن تكتسب القياس فضع الحدين أعني الأصغر والأكبر، واطلب حد كل واحد منهما وكل ما يمكن حمله على كل واحد منهما من الأنواع والأجناس والفصول والخواص والعوارض وما يحمل على كل واحد من هذه الخمسة من الأنواع والأجناس والفصول والخواص وأعراضها. وقد علمت من قبل أن لكل واحد منها أنواعًا وأجناسًا وفصولاً وخواص وأعراضًا وهي لواحق اللواحق. وعلى هذا مرة بعد أخرى إلى أن قدرت. فإن جميع ذلك محمول على الحد ثم يطلب جميع ما يلحقه كل واحد من الحدين المذكورين بالنسبة التي مرَّ ذكرها وهي الأمور التي يكون الحد نوعًا لها أو جنسًا أو فصلا أو خاصة أو عرضًا عاماً كذلك. وكذلك بهذه النسبة مرة بعد أخرى إلى أن قدرت؛ فهذه مواد طلب الإيجاب. وأما مواد السلب فاطلب أيضًا جميع الأمور التي لا يوجد لحد حد منها ضرورة أو إطلاقا. ولا تشتغل بطلب ما لا يلحقه حد حد، فإن الذي لا يلحقه حد حد هو نفس ما لا يلحق حدًا حدا. وأما ما يلحق فليس هو نفس ما يلحق فإن الموضوعات التي على المجرى الطبعي تمايز المحمولات التي على المجرى الطبعي وإن دخل بعضها في بعض إذا كانت على غير المجرى الطبعي. ثم لقائل أن يقول كيف هو ولا يلزم من سلب الشيء عن غيره سلب ذلك الغير عنه. ألا ترى أن الخواص المفارقة للشيء من جملة ما يصح سلبه عن ذلك الشيء ولا يصح سلب ذلك الشيء عن شيء من الخواص ؟ لكنا نقول : إذا صح قولنا هذا الشيء ليس ذلك صح قولنا ذلك الشيء ليس هذا. فإن من المحال أن يكون هذا من حيث إنه هو غير ذلك الشيء، وإن ذلك الشيء من حيث هو هو نفس هذا. نعم يمكن أن يسلب الشيء عن الشيء بحسب التحقق، ولا يمكن أن يسلب ذلك الشيء عنه بحسب التحقق كما في الخواص المفارقة.
Then if you want to acquire the syllogism, put the two terms, namely the minor and the major, and seek the definition of each of them and everything that can be predicated of each of them among species, genera, differentiae, properties, and accidents, and what is predicated of each of these five among species, genera, differentiae, properties, and their accidents. You have known before that each of them has species, genera, differentiae, properties, and accidents, and they are concomitants of concomitants. And so on repeatedly until you are able. For all of that is predicated of the term. Then seek all that each of the mentioned two terms is attached to according to the relation that was mentioned, which are the things for which the term is a species, or genus, or differentia, or property, or general accident. And likewise according to this relation repeatedly until you are able. These are the materials of affirmation. As for the materials of negation, also seek all things of which a term necessarily or absolutely does not exist. And do not occupy yourself with seeking what is not attached to a term at all, for what is not attached to a term is exactly what is not attached to a term. But what is attached is not exactly what is attached, for the subjects according to the natural course are distinct from predicates according to the natural course, even if some enter into others when they are not according to the natural course. Then someone may say: How is that? For it is not necessary that from the negation of a thing about another the negation of that other about it follows. Do you not see that distinguishing properties of a thing are among what can be negated of that thing, but that thing cannot be negated of any of the properties? But we say: If it is correct to say 'this thing is not that', it is correct to say 'that thing is not this'. For it is impossible that this, insofar as it is, is other than that thing, and that thing, insofar as it is, is the same as this. Yes, it is possible to negate a thing of a thing with respect to actual existence, and it is not possible to negate that other thing of it with respect to actual existence, as in the case of distinguishing properties.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a practical guide for constructing a syllogism. The author instructs the student to first identify the two extremes (minor term subject and major term predicate of the conclusion) and then systematically search for every term that can be predicated of each extreme, including their definitions, genera, species, differentiae, properties, and accidents. This process is recursive: after finding predicates of the extremes, one should also find the predicates of those predicates (the 'concomitants of concomitants'), repeating until one has a sufficient stock of terms. These collected predicates constitute the 'materials of affirmation' (i.e., potential middle terms). For the 'materials of negation', one must also list everything that is necessarily or absolutely not predicated of each extreme. The author warns against wasting time on terms that are not attached to either extreme at all (i.e., irrelevant). He then addresses a possible objection: if we say 'A is not B', does it follow that 'B is not A'? The objector points out that a property (like 'laughing' for a human) can be negated of the human (not all humans laugh), but the human cannot be negated of the property (you cannot say 'laughing is not human' in the same sense). The author responds that in terms of logical predication (the relationship of identity), the negation is reciprocal: if A is not B in essence, then B is not A in essence. However, in terms of actual existence (contingent instantiation), it is possible to have non-reciprocal negation, as in the case of properties that accidentally belong to the subject.

Key Takeaway
Constructing a syllogism requires a recursive exhaustive search of all predicates and negatives of the terms.

The Reciprocity of Negation: Essential vs. Existential Negation
Author discusses the reciprocity of negation and the distinction between essential and existential negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential (logical) negation and existential (actual) negation, and identify when negation is reciprocal.

Core Idea
Negation of terms is reciprocal in essence but not always in existence.

The author addresses a subtle point: if we negate a property of a subject, does it follow that the subject can be negated of the property? For example, 'human is not laughing' does not entail 'laughing is not human' because the property is accidental. The author clarifies that in essence the negation is reciprocal, but in actual existence it may not be.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objector's claim
The objector says: From 'A is not B' it does not necessarily follow that 'B is not A'. The example: a property like 'laughing' can be negated of a human (since not all humans laugh), but the human cannot be negated of 'laughing' because 'laughing' is not a subject that can possess 'human'.
2
Analyze the author's response: essential negation
The author says: In terms of logical identity (essence), if 'this thing is not that' then 'that thing is not this'. This is because it is impossible for two things to be other than each other in essence while one of them is the same as the other.
The author's words: 'فإن من المحال أن يكون هذا من حيث إنه هو غير ذلك الشيء، وإن ذلك الشيء من حيث هو هو نفس هذا.'
3
Apply the distinction between essential and existential negation
The author concedes that in terms of actual existence (تحقق), negation may not be reciprocal. For example, a property that is contingent (like 'laughing' for humans) can be negated of the human, but the human cannot be negated of the property because the property depends on the subject.
Essential negation is about the very identity; existential negation is about actual occurrence.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Consider: 'Khamr is not halal' in Islamic law. In essential negation, if khamr is not halal in its very nature (it is forbidden in essence? Actually no, it is forbidden by law, not by essence. But the logical point: if it is true that 'No khamr is halal' then it follows 'No halal is khamr' in essence. But existentially, a particular instance of something considered halal might not be khamr.
This distinction is crucial in legal reasoning.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Focus on Universal Concomitants and Avoid Sterile Searches
Author advises to seek universal predicates only and warns against searches that yield no conclusion in figures two, three, and four.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify universal concomitants and recognize which syllogistic searches (e.g., both affirmatives in second figure) are unproductive.

Core Idea
Only universal concomitants yield fruitful syllogisms; many common searches lead nowhere.

In building a syllogism, not every term relation is worth pursuing. The author advises the student to focus only on universal concomitants—those that apply to the whole subject—and to avoid several types of searches that are known to be sterile due to the rules of the figures. This lesson will help you internalize these practical heuristics so that you do not waste effort on fruitless logical pathways.

Guided Path
1
Understand the universal concomitant
A universal concomitant is a term that accompanies the whole subject, not merely some individuals. For example, 'animal' is a universal concomitant of 'human' because it applies to all humans. Identify such terms in your syllogism.
The author says 'ليس اللاحق الكلي ما يلحق بكلية الموضوع بل ما يلحق كلية الموضوع' meaning it accompanies the subject in its entirety.
2
Distinguish beneficial from useless searches
Seek only universal concomitants and universal accompanied things. Avoid searches for terms that accompany both extremes or neither, as they do not contribute to a valid syllogism.
The passage says: 'ولا عليك أن تشتغل بطلب اللواحق التي تلحق الطرفين والتي لا تلحق الطرفين'.
3
Recognize the sterile combination in the second figure
If both premises are affirmative in the second figure (e.g., 'All A are B, all C are B'), no conclusion follows because the middle term is predicate in both and there is no negative premise to differentiate.
Example: 'All humans are animals, all dogs are animals' tells you nothing about humans and dogs.
4
Recognize the invalid negative minor in the second figure
If the minor premise is negative in the second figure (e.g., 'Some B is not A, all B are C'), the syllogism yields no conclusion. The negative minor violates the figure's conditions.
The passage says: 'فإن الصغرى السالبة في الشكل الثاني لا تنتج'.
5
Recognize the invalid negative minor in the third figure
Similarly, a negative minor premise in the third figure (e.g., 'No B is A, some B are C') is sterile. The third figure requires a particular conclusion, and a negative minor disrupts validity.
The passage says: 'فإن الصغرى السالبة في الشكل الثالث لا تنتج'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنك إذا أمعنت النظر في الإستكثار من هذه اللواحق والملحوقات وغيرها فأنتَ أقرب من إصابة الغرض. واللواحق التي تلحق غير الملحوق الكلي مما لا ينتفع به في أكثر الأمر بل عليك باقتناص اللواحق الكلية والملحوقات الكلية. وليس اللاحق الكلي ما يلحق بكلية الموضوع بل ما يلحق كلية الموضوع : ولا عليك أن تشتغل بطلب اللواحق التي تلحق الطرفين والتي لا تلحق الطرفين. فإن ذلك لا يفيد لما مر من قبل أن المتفقتين في الكيف لا تنتجان في الشكل الثاني. وكذلك لا يفيد اشتغالك بطلب أن محمول المطلوب هل يحمل على شيء هو مسلوب عن موضوعه؛ فإن الصغرى السالبة في الشكل الثاني لا تنتج. وكذلك لا يفيد طلب أن ما هو محمول المطلوب هل يحمل على شيء لا يحمل موضوعه عليه؛ فإن الصغرى السالبة في الشكل الثالث لا تنتج. وعلى هذا بالنسبة إلى ما لا ينتج نحو السالبة الجزئية في الشكل الرابع: فإن الاشتغال بطلبه اشتغال بما لا يعنيه.
Then if you deeply reflect on seeking many of these concomitants and those they accompany and others, you will be closer to hitting the mark. The concomitants that accompany other than the universal accompanied are not beneficial in most cases; rather you must catch universal concomitants and universal accompanied things. And the universal concomitant is not that which accompanies every part of the subject, but that which accompanies the whole subject. And do not occupy yourself with seeking concomitants that accompany both extremes or that do not accompany both extremes. For that is not beneficial, as has passed: that two [premises] agreeing in quality do not produce a conclusion in the second figure. Likewise, your occupation with seeking whether the predicate of the desired conclusion is predicated of something that is negated of its subject is not beneficial; for the negative minor premise in the second figure does not yield a conclusion. Likewise, seeking whether what is the predicate of the desired conclusion is predicated of something that its subject is not predicated of is not beneficial; for the negative minor premise in the third figure does not yield a conclusion. And according to this, with respect to what does not yield a conclusion like the particular negative in the fourth figure: occupying oneself with seeking it is an occupation with what does not concern him.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage provides practical advice for efficiently constructing syllogisms. The author emphasizes that one should focus on universal concomitants—those that apply to the subject as a whole—rather than accidental or non-universal ones. He then gives three specific warnings: (1) Do not waste time seeking terms that apply to both extremes equally or to neither, because if both premises are affirmative in the second figure, no conclusion follows (e.g., 'All A are B, all C are B' gives no necessary relation between A and C). (2) Do not try to find a term that is predicated of something that is negated of the subject, because a negative minor in the second figure is invalid (e.g., 'Some B is not A, all B are C' does not yield a valid conclusion about A and C). (3) Similarly, do not seek a term that is predicated of something that the subject is not predicated of, because a negative minor in the third figure is invalid (e.g., 'No B is A, some B are C' yields nothing about A and C). (4) Also avoid seeking a particular negative in the fourth figure, as it is known that the fourth figure cannot produce a particular negative conclusion (it produces only universal conclusions). The author's point is that the student should only invest effort in searches that can actually yield a valid syllogism, avoiding those that are known to be sterile due to the rules of the figures.

شكل ثاني
شكل ثالث

C) Seeking a term that is negated of the subject in the first figure.
D) Seeking a universal predicate that applies to the major term.

Key Takeaway
Only universal concomitants that apply to the whole subject are worth seeking; searches based on both premises being affirmative in the second figure, or negative minor in second or third figure, or particular negative in fourth figure, are sterile and should be avoided.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Determining Syllogism Figures from Attachments
Author gives detailed conditions for forming valid categorical syllogisms in different figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which figure of categorical syllogism is formed given a desired conclusion type and the relations between the attachments (lawāḥiq) and attached things (malḥūqāt).

Core Idea
The figure of a syllogism is determined by the logical relation between the conclusion and the middle term found in attachments and attached things.

In this passage, the author provides a practical algorithm for constructing syllogisms once you have identified the universal predicates (attachments) and their subjects (attached things). By examining whether the attachments of the subject are also attached to the predicate (or not), you can directly read off which of the four figures is valid for your desired conclusion. This is a parsing exercise that sharpens your ability to recognize syllogistic structure in theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify conclusion type
Determine whether the desired conclusion is universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. The passage separately treats each type.
The conclusion type dictates which conditions to check.
2
Universal affirmative condition
If the conclusion is universal affirmative, look for an attachment (lawāḥiq) of the subject that is also an attached thing (malḥūqāt) of the predicate by affirmation. If found, a first-figure syllogism is formed.
The middle term is that shared attachment.
3
Universal negative alternatives
For a universal negative conclusion, first check if an attachment of the subject is an attached thing of the predicate by negation (first figure). If not, check if an attachment of each individual of one term is negated of each individual of the other term (second figure).
These correspond to different moods in the first and second figures.
4
Particular affirmative conditions
For a particular affirmative, the passage lists three conditions: first figure (attachments of predicate are attached things of some subject individuals), third figure (attachments of both terms are attached things of each other), and fourth figure (attachments of predicate are attached things of subject).
Each condition yields a valid mood in the respective figure.
5
Particular negative conditions
For a particular negative, two conditions are given: first figure (attachments of some subject individuals are negated of each predicate individual) and second figure (attachments of each predicate individual are attachments of some subject individuals by negation, or attachments of each predicate individual by affirmation are not attachments of some subject individuals).
These cover the valid moods for particular negative conclusions in those figures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذا حصل كل ما قلناه من اللواحق والملحوقات فعليك أن تتأمل : فإن كان مطلوبك إيجابًا كليًّا، وقد وجدت في لواحق الموضوع هو من ملحوقات المحمول بالإيجاب فقد انعقد قياس من الشكل الأول. وإن كان مطلوبك سلبًا كليًّا، وقد وجدت في لواحق الموضوع ما هو من ملحوقات المحمول بالسلب فقد انعقد قياس من الشكل الأول أيضًا. وإن وجدت في لواحق كل واحد من آحاد أحد الطرفين بالإيجاب ما يسلب عن كل واحد من آحاد الطرف الآخر فقد انعقد قياس من الشكل الثاني. وإن وجدت في لواحق كل واحد من آحاد المحمول بالإيجاب ما هو من ملحوقات الموضوع بالسلب فقد انعقد من الشكل الرابع. وإن كان المطلوب إيجابًا جزئيا، وقد وجدت في ملحوقات كل واحد من آحاد المحمول ما يكون من لواحق البعض من آحاد الموضوع فقد انعقد من الشكل الأول. وإن وجدت في ملحوقات كل واحد من آحاد طرفي المطلوب أو في البعض منها بالإيجاب ما يكون من ملحوقات كل واحد من آحاد الطرف الآخر بالإيجاب فقد انعقد من الشكل الثالث. وإن وجدت في لواحق كل واحد من آحاد المحمول أو في البعض منها بالإيجاب ما يكون من ملحوقات كل واحد من آحاد الموضوع فقد انعقد من الشكل الرابع. وإن كان المطلوب سلبًا جزئيّاً وقد وجدت في لواحق البعض من الموضوع بالإيجاب ما يسلب عن كل واحد من آحاد المحمول فقد انعقد من الشكل الأول. وإن وجدت في لواحق كل واحد من آحاد المحمول بالسلب ما يكون من لواحق البعض من آحاد الموضوع أو وجدت في لواحق كل واحد من المحمول بالإيجاب ما لا يكون من لواحق البعض من الموضوع فقد انعقد من الشكل الثاني.
Then, when you have obtained all that we have said of attachments and attached things, you must reflect: if your desired conclusion is a universal affirmative, and you find that among the attachments of the subject is something that is among the attached things of the predicate by affirmation, then a syllogism from the first figure is formed. If your desired conclusion is a universal negative, and you find that among the attachments of the subject is something that is among the attached things of the predicate by negation, then a syllogism from the first figure is also formed. If you find that, among the attachments of each individual of one of the two terms by affirmation, something is negated of each individual of the other term, then a syllogism from the second figure is formed. If you find that, among the attachments of each individual of the predicate by affirmation, something is among the attached things of the subject by negation, then it is formed from the fourth figure. If the desired conclusion is a particular affirmative, and you find that among the attached things of each individual of the predicate is something that is among the attachments of some individuals of the subject, then a syllogism from the first figure is formed. If you find that among the attached things of each individual of the two terms of the desired conclusion or in some of them by affirmation is something that is among the attached things of each individual of the other term by affirmation, then it is formed from the third figure. If you find that among the attachments of each individual of the predicate or in some of them by affirmation is something that is among the attached things of each individual of the subject, then it is formed from the fourth figure. If the desired conclusion is a particular negative, and you find that among the attachments of some individuals of the subject by affirmation is something that is negated of each individual of the predicate, then a syllogism from the first figure is formed. If you find that among the attachments of each individual of the predicate by negation is something that is among the attachments of some individuals of the subject, or you find that among the attachments of each individual of the predicate by affirmation is something that is not among the attachments of some individuals of the subject, then it is formed from the second figure.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage provides practical instructions for constructing categorical syllogisms once one has collected the universal attachments (lawāḥiq) and attached things (malḥūqāt). It is a step-by-step enumeration of conditions for each type of conclusion. The author first considers universal conclusions. For a universal affirmative (umumī ījābī), if some attachment of the subject is also an attached thing of the predicate by affirmation, a first-figure syllogism is formed (e.g., if subject's attachment B is such that predicate C is also an attachment of B, then B is the middle term). For a universal negative, similarly but with negation in the relation. Then for the second figure: if an attachment of each individual of one term is negated of each individual of the other term, a second-figure syllogism is formed. For the fourth figure: if an attachment of each individual of the predicate is an attached thing of the subject by negation. The author then treats particular conclusions. For particular affirmative, three possibilities: first figure (based on attached things of predicate being attachments of some subject individuals), third figure (based on attached things of both terms agreeing), and fourth figure (attachments of predicate being attached things of subject). For particular negative: first figure (based on attachments of some subject individuals being negated of each predicate individual), and second figure (based on attachments of each predicate individual either being attachments of some subject individuals by negation, or attachments of each predicate individual by affirmation not being attachments of some subject individuals). Each condition corresponds to a valid mood in the respective figure. The passage assumes familiarity with the figures and their valid moods. The key is that the attachments and attached things serve as middle terms.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Determining the Figure from Subject and Predicate Attributes
Rules for identifying syllogistic figures by attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the figure of a categorical syllogism by analyzing the attributes of the subject and predicate of the desired conclusion according to the rules given in the passage.

Core Idea
The figure is revealed by how an attribute of the subject relates to the predicate.

This lesson presents a practical rule for identifying the figure of a categorical syllogism without explicitly writing the premises. By checking whether an attribute of the subject is affirmed or denied of the predicate, and considering the quantity and quality of the conclusion, you can immediately determine which figure the syllogism belongs to. The passage enumerates all combinations for universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate of the desired conclusion
The desired conclusion (al-maṭlūb) has a subject (mawḍūʿ) and a predicate (maḥmūl). Note its quantity (universal or particular) and quality (affirmative or negative).
2
Search for an attribute of the subject that is also attributed to the predicate
Examine the attributes (lawāḥiq) of the subject and see if any of them are among the things attributed (malḥūqāt) to the predicate, either affirmatively or negatively.
3
Apply the matching rule for universal affirmative conclusions
If the conclusion is universal affirmative and you find an attribute of the subject that is affirmed of the predicate, then the syllogism is in the first figure.
Example: 'Every intoxicant is haram' – the attribute 'intoxicating' is affirmed of the predicate 'haram'.
4
Apply the rules for universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative
For universal negative, if the attribute of the subject is denied of every predicate individual, it is first figure. For particular affirmative, look for an attribute of some predicate individuals that is among the attributes of some subject individuals; that yields first figure. For particular negative, an attribute of some subject individuals denied of every predicate gives first figure. Other patterns yield second, third, or fourth figures as listed.
Refer to the full enumeration in the source passage.
5
Verify the middle term is a single concept
Confirm that the identified attribute serves as a single middle term (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ) connecting the premises. The middle term must be one thing, not two opposites.

ثم إذا حصل كل ما قلناه من اللواحق والملحوقات فعليك أن تتأمل : فإن كان مطلوبك إيجابًا كليًّا، وقد وجدت في لواحق الموضوع هو من ملحوقات المحمول بالإيجاب فقد انعقد قياس من الشكل الأول. وإن كان مطلوبك سلبًا كليًّا، وقد وجدت في لواحق الموضوع ما هو من ملحوقات المحمول بالسلب فقد انعقد قياس من الشكل الأول أيضًا. وإن وجدت في لواحق كل واحد من آحاد أحد الطرفين بالإيجاب ما يسلب عن كل واحد من آحاد الطرف الآخر فقد انعقد قياس من الشكل الثاني. وإن وجدت في لواحق كل واحد من آحاد المحمول بالإيجاب ما هو من ملحوقات الموضوع بالسلب فقد انعقد من الشكل الرابع. وإن كان المطلوب إيجابًا جزئيا، وقد وجدت في ملحوقات كل واحد من آحاد المحمول ما يكون من لواحق البعض من آحاد الموضوع فقد انعقد من الشكل الأول. وإن وجدت في ملحوقات كل واحد من آحاد طرفي المطلوب أو في البعض منها بالإيجاب ما يكون من ملحوقات كل واحد من آحاد الطرف الآخر بالإيجاب فقد انعقد من الشكل الثالث. وإن وجدت في لواحق كل واحد من آحاد المحمول أو في البعض منها بالإيجاب ما يكون من ملحوقات كل واحد من آحاد الموضوع فقد انعقد من الشكل الرابع. وإن كان المطلوب سلبًا جزئيّاً وقد وجدت في لواحق البعض من الموضوع بالإيجاب ما يسلب عن كل واحد من آحاد المحمول فقد انعقد من الشكل الأول. وإن وجدت في لواحق كل واحد من آحاد المحمول بالسلب ما يكون من لواحق البعض من آحاد الموضوع أو وجدت في لواحق كل واحد من المحمول بالإيجاب ما لا يكون من لواحق البعض من الموضوع فقد انعقد من الشكل الثاني. وإن وجدت في ملحوقات كل واحد من آحاد الموضوع بالإيجاب ما يسلب عن جميع آحاد الموضوع أو عن بعض آحاد محموله أو وجدت في ملحوقات البعض من آحاد الموضوع بالإيجاب ما يسلب عن جميع آحاد المحمول فقد انعقد من الشكل الثالث. وإن وجدت ما يحمل على كل واحد من آحاد الموضوع أو على البعض منها بالإيجاب ويسلب عن كل واحد من آحاد المحمول فقد انعقد من الشكل الرابع ، والمراد من الموضوع والمحمول فيما ذكرناه موضوع المطلوب ومحموله.

ثم إنك إذا امتحنت حال ما يلحق وما لا يلحق فلك أن تبتدئ من أعم لواحق أحدهما هل هو ما لا يلحق ؟ فإنه إذا كان غير لاحق كفيت المعونة، وعلمت أن ما دونه غير لاحق وإن كان لاحقا فاللازم أن ينزل عنه درجة. ثمَّ تلك الدرجة على الولاء، فإن فيه سرعة لإصابة ومصادفة القياس الأول. فإن سلب الناطق عن البياض ليس سلبًا واليا بل سلب الجسم عنه أو الجوهر، فإذا كنتَ في طلب هذا الامتحان فلا يكون قصارى طلبك أنه هل في لواحق الحدين شيء مضاد للواحق الحد الآخر أو مغاير حتى تقول مثلا إن "ج بارد وأ حار، وذلك لأن الحد الأوسط يجب أن يكون شيئًا واحدا. وأما ههنا فإن الأوسط اثنان؛ وإن ما يمكنك ترتيبه قياسًا واحدا أكبر من قياس واحد بالضرورة. وذلك لأن «أ» ليس مسلوبًا حينئذ عن «ج ب» سبب كونه موصوفا بشيء هو ضد ما يوصف به ذلك حتى يكون هذا هو الذي لأجله ينعقد القياس المنتج للسلب فإنه إذا كان بدل الضد مضاف أو عدم أو ملكة أو ما يكون غيره كان القياس ينعقد فالبارد؛ إذ الحق (ج) كان قياسه إلى «أ» قياسان؛ أحدهما أنه غير لاحق بــه والآخر أنه ضد لاحقه. وإنما ينعقد منه القياس لأنه غير لاحق فقط . فإنك إن حفظت أنه غير لاحق وبدلت كونه مضادا للاحق استمر القياس المطلوب. وعلى هذا في سائر ما يكون من هذا القبيل؛ فالخلف
Then, when everything we have said about the attributes and the things that are attributed is obtained, you must reflect: if your desired conclusion is a universal affirmative, and you have found among the attributes of the subject something that is among the things attributed to the predicate in an affirmative way, then a syllogism of the first figure is formed. And if your desired conclusion is a universal negative, and you have found among the attributes of the subject something that is among the things attributed to the predicate in a negative way, then a syllogism of the first figure is also formed. And if you have found among the attributes of every single individual of one extreme in an affirmative way what is denied of every single individual of the other extreme, then a syllogism of the second figure is formed. And if you have found among the attributes of every single individual of the predicate in an affirmative way what is among the things attributed to the subject in a negative way, then it is formed from the fourth figure. And if the desired conclusion is a particular affirmative, and you have found among the things attributed to every single individual of the predicate what is among the attributes of some individuals of the subject, then it is formed from the first figure. And if you have found among the things attributed to every single individual of the two extremes of the desired conclusion, or in some of them in an affirmative way, what is among the things attributed to every single individual of the other extreme in an affirmative way, then it is formed from the third figure. And if you have found among the attributes of every single individual of the predicate, or in some of them in an affirmative way, what is among the things attributed to every single individual of the subject, then it is formed from the fourth figure. And if the desired conclusion is a particular negative, and you have found among the attributes of some individuals of the subject in an affirmative way what is denied of every single individual of the predicate, then it is formed from the first figure. And if you have found among the attributes of every single individual of the predicate in a negative way what is among the attributes of some individuals of the subject, or you have found among the attributes of every single individual of the predicate in an affirmative way what is not among the attributes of some individuals of the subject, then it is formed from the second figure. And if you have found among the things attributed to every single individual of the subject in an affirmative way what is denied of all individuals of the subject or of some individuals of its predicate, or you have found among the things attributed to some individuals of the subject in an affirmative way what is denied of all individuals of the predicate, then it is formed from the third figure. And if you have found what is predicated of every single individual of the subject or of some of them in an affirmative way and is denied of every single individual of the predicate, then it is formed from the fourth figure. And by subject and predicate in what we have mentioned, we mean the subject and predicate of the desired conclusion.

Then, if you examine the state of what is attributed and what is not attributed, you may begin from the most general attribute of one of them: is it something that is not attributed? For if it is not attributed, it suffices for the assistance, and you know that what is below it is also not attributed. And if it is attributed, then it is necessary to descend from it by one degree. Then those degrees in succession, for in it there is speed for correctness and obtaining the first syllogism. For the negation of 'rational' from 'whiteness' is not an absolute negation, but rather the negation of 'body' from it or of 'substance'. So if you are seeking this examination, your utmost request should not be whether among the attributes of the two limits there is something opposite to the attributes of the other limit or different, such that you say, for example, 'J is cold and A is hot,' because the middle term must be one thing. But here the middle is two; and what you can arrange as one syllogism is necessarily larger than one syllogism. That is because 'A' is not denied of 'J' due to its being described by something that is the opposite of what it is described by, so that this would be the reason for the formation of the syllogism that produces the negative. For if instead of the opposite there were a relation, privation, possession, or something else, the syllogism would be formed. So the cold; indeed the truth (J) had two relations to 'A': one that it is not attributed to it, and the other that it is the opposite of what is attributed to it. But the syllogism is formed from it only because it is not attributed. So if you preserve that it is not attributed and you replace its being opposite to what is attributed, the desired syllogism continues. And this applies to everything of this kind; thus the reverse.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage provides a practical algorithm for constructing a categorical syllogism in any of the four figures by examining the 'lawāḥiq' (attributes) of the subject and the 'malḥūqāt' (things attributed to) the predicate of the desired conclusion. The author first enumerates all possible cases for a universal affirmative conclusion: if you find an attribute of the subject that is also affirmed of the predicate, you get the first figure. For a universal negative: if that attribute is denied of the predicate, again the first figure. For the second figure: you find an attribute of one extreme that is denied of every individual of the other extreme via a common predicate. For the fourth figure: an attribute of every predicate individual that is denied of the subject. For particular affirmatives: similar but with existential quantification. The author then explains a method to efficiently test for the middle term by starting with the most general attribute: if it is not an attribute, then all subsumed ones also are not; if it is an attribute, descend stepwise to find the right level. He gives an example: 'the negation of rational from whiteness' is not direct but via body or substance. A critical point is that one must not confuse opposition with mere non-attribution; the middle term must be a single thing. Opposites would require two syllogisms. The key is that the negation is due to non-attribution, not contradiction. Therefore, when seeking a negative conclusion, you should look for a middle that is simply not attributed, not one that is opposite. The passage concludes by stating that this rule applies analogously to other cases.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Efficient Search for the Middle Term and Avoiding the Opposite Fallacy
Method for efficient middle term search and warning against opposites

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to efficiently test candidate middle terms by starting from the broadest attribute and avoid the error of mistaking an opposite for a simple non-attribution.

Core Idea
Start broad; if the broadest attribute fails, all narrower ones also fail; do not confuse opposition with absence.

After determining the general pattern for the figure, you need to find the actual middle term. The author provides a method: begin with the most general attribute of one extreme. If that attribute does not apply to the other extreme, then all narrower attributes also do not apply, saving effort. However, a critical warning is given: do not mistake an opposite attribute for a simple non-attribution. If you treat an opposite as a non-attribution, you will end up with two middle terms (the attribute and its opposite), which cannot yield a single syllogism. The middle term must be one concept.

Guided Path
1
Identify the predicate of the conclusion for negation or affirmation
When seeking a negative conclusion, focus on the predicate that you want to deny of the subject. You will look for an attribute of the subject that is absent from the predicate.
2
Start with the broadest (most general) attribute of the predicate
Consider the most general attribute (أعم اللواحق) of the predicate. For example, for 'stone', the broadest attribute might be 'body' or 'substance'.
3
Check if this broad attribute is attributed to the subject
If the broad attribute is not attributed to the subject, then you know that all narrower attributes are also not attributed, and you have a candidate middle term. If it is attributed, you must descend to a less general attribute until you find one that is not attributed.
This iterative process saves time.
4
Ensure the non-attribution is not due to opposition
Do not treat an opposite attribute (e.g., 'hot' vs 'cold') as a simple non-attribution. If you say 'J is cold and A is hot', the middle term is not one but two: cold and hot. The negation in the conclusion should be based on the absence of a single attribute, not on the presence of an opposite.
The author warns: 'the middle term must be one thing.'
5
Construct the syllogism with the selected middle term
Once you have a candidate attribute that is simply not attributed to the subject (or predicate), use it as the middle term in the appropriate figure. The syllogism should now be valid.

ثم إذا حصل كل ما قلناه من اللواحق والملحوقات فعليك أن تتأمل : فإن كان مطلوبك إيجابًا كليًّا، وقد وجدت في لواحق الموضوع هو من ملحوقات المحمول بالإيجاب فقد انعقد قياس من الشكل الأول. وإن كان مطلوبك سلبًا كليًّا، وقد وجدت في لواحق الموضوع ما هو من ملحوقات المحمول بالسلب فقد انعقد قياس من الشكل الأول أيضًا. وإن وجدت في لواحق كل واحد من آحاد أحد الطرفين بالإيجاب ما يسلب عن كل واحد من آحاد الطرف الآخر فقد انعقد قياس من الشكل الثاني. وإن وجدت في لواحق كل واحد من آحاد المحمول بالإيجاب ما هو من ملحوقات الموضوع بالسلب فقد انعقد من الشكل الرابع. وإن كان المطلوب إيجابًا جزئيا، وقد وجدت في ملحوقات كل واحد من آحاد المحمول ما يكون من لواحق البعض من آحاد الموضوع فقد انعقد من الشكل الأول. وإن وجدت في ملحوقات كل واحد من آحاد طرفي المطلوب أو في البعض منها بالإيجاب ما يكون من ملحوقات كل واحد من آحاد الطرف الآخر بالإيجاب فقد انعقد من الشكل الثالث. وإن وجدت في لواحق كل واحد من آحاد المحمول أو في البعض منها بالإيجاب ما يكون من ملحوقات كل واحد من آحاد الموضوع فقد انعقد من الشكل الرابع. وإن كان المطلوب سلبًا جزئيّاً وقد وجدت في لواحق البعض من الموضوع بالإيجاب ما يسلب عن كل واحد من آحاد المحمول فقد انعقد من الشكل الأول. وإن وجدت في لواحق كل واحد من آحاد المحمول بالسلب ما يكون من لواحق البعض من آحاد الموضوع أو وجدت في لواحق كل واحد من المحمول بالإيجاب ما لا يكون من لواحق البعض من الموضوع فقد انعقد من الشكل الثاني. وإن وجدت في ملحوقات كل واحد من آحاد الموضوع بالإيجاب ما يسلب عن جميع آحاد الموضوع أو عن بعض آحاد محموله أو وجدت في ملحوقات البعض من آحاد الموضوع بالإيجاب ما يسلب عن جميع آحاد المحمول فقد انعقد من الشكل الثالث. وإن وجدت ما يحمل على كل واحد من آحاد الموضوع أو على البعض منها بالإيجاب ويسلب عن كل واحد من آحاد المحمول فقد انعقد من الشكل الرابع ، والمراد من الموضوع والمحمول فيما ذكرناه موضوع المطلوب ومحموله.

ثم إنك إذا امتحنت حال ما يلحق وما لا يلحق فلك أن تبتدئ من أعم لواحق أحدهما هل هو ما لا يلحق ؟ فإنه إذا كان غير لاحق كفيت المعونة، وعلمت أن ما دونه غير لاحق وإن كان لاحقا فاللازم أن ينزل عنه درجة. ثمَّ تلك الدرجة على الولاء، فإن فيه سرعة لإصابة ومصادفة القياس الأول. فإن سلب الناطق عن البياض ليس سلبًا واليا بل سلب الجسم عنه أو الجوهر، فإذا كنتَ في طلب هذا الامتحان فلا يكون قصارى طلبك أنه هل في لواحق الحدين شيء مضاد للواحق الحد الآخر أو مغاير حتى تقول مثلا إن "ج بارد وأ حار، وذلك لأن الحد الأوسط يجب أن يكون شيئًا واحدا. وأما ههنا فإن الأوسط اثنان؛ وإن ما يمكنك ترتيبه قياسًا واحدا أكبر من قياس واحد بالضرورة. وذلك لأن «أ» ليس مسلوبًا حينئذ عن «ج ب» سبب كونه موصوفا بشيء هو ضد ما يوصف به ذلك حتى يكون هذا هو الذي لأجله ينعقد القياس المنتج للسلب فإنه إذا كان بدل الضد مضاف أو عدم أو ملكة أو ما يكون غيره كان القياس ينعقد فالبارد؛ إذ الحق (ج) كان قياسه إلى «أ» قياسان؛ أحدهما أنه غير لاحق بــه والآخر أنه ضد لاحقه. وإنما ينعقد منه القياس لأنه غير لاحق فقط . فإنك إن حفظت أنه غير لاحق وبدلت كونه مضادا للاحق استمر القياس المطلوب. وعلى هذا في سائر ما يكون من هذا القبيل؛ فالخلف
Then, when everything we have said about the attributes and the things that are attributed is obtained, you must reflect: if your desired conclusion is a universal affirmative, and you have found among the attributes of the subject something that is among the things attributed to the predicate in an affirmative way, then a syllogism of the first figure is formed. And if your desired conclusion is a universal negative, and you have found among the attributes of the subject something that is among the things attributed to the predicate in a negative way, then a syllogism of the first figure is also formed. And if you have found among the attributes of every single individual of one extreme in an affirmative way what is denied of every single individual of the other extreme, then a syllogism of the second figure is formed. And if you have found among the attributes of every single individual of the predicate in an affirmative way what is among the things attributed to the subject in a negative way, then it is formed from the fourth figure. And if the desired conclusion is a particular affirmative, and you have found among the things attributed to every single individual of the predicate what is among the attributes of some individuals of the subject, then it is formed from the first figure. And if you have found among the things attributed to every single individual of the two extremes of the desired conclusion, or in some of them in an affirmative way, what is among the things attributed to every single individual of the other extreme in an affirmative way, then it is formed from the third figure. And if you have found among the attributes of every single individual of the predicate, or in some of them in an affirmative way, what is among the things attributed to every single individual of the subject, then it is formed from the fourth figure. And if the desired conclusion is a particular negative, and you have found among the attributes of some individuals of the subject in an affirmative way what is denied of every single individual of the predicate, then it is formed from the first figure. And if you have found among the attributes of every single individual of the predicate in a negative way what is among the attributes of some individuals of the subject, or you have found among the attributes of every single individual of the predicate in an affirmative way what is not among the attributes of some individuals of the subject, then it is formed from the second figure. And if you have found among the things attributed to every single individual of the subject in an affirmative way what is denied of all individuals of the subject or of some individuals of its predicate, or you have found among the things attributed to some individuals of the subject in an affirmative way what is denied of all individuals of the predicate, then it is formed from the third figure. And if you have found what is predicated of every single individual of the subject or of some of them in an affirmative way and is denied of every single individual of the predicate, then it is formed from the fourth figure. And by subject and predicate in what we have mentioned, we mean the subject and predicate of the desired conclusion.

Then, if you examine the state of what is attributed and what is not attributed, you may begin from the most general attribute of one of them: is it something that is not attributed? For if it is not attributed, it suffices for the assistance, and you know that what is below it is also not attributed. And if it is attributed, then it is necessary to descend from it by one degree. Then those degrees in succession, for in it there is speed for correctness and obtaining the first syllogism. For the negation of 'rational' from 'whiteness' is not an absolute negation, but rather the negation of 'body' from it or of 'substance'. So if you are seeking this examination, your utmost request should not be whether among the attributes of the two limits there is something opposite to the attributes of the other limit or different, such that you say, for example, 'J is cold and A is hot,' because the middle term must be one thing. But here the middle is two; and what you can arrange as one syllogism is necessarily larger than one syllogism. That is because 'A' is not denied of 'J' due to its being described by something that is the opposite of what it is described by, so that this would be the reason for the formation of the syllogism that produces the negative. For if instead of the opposite there were a relation, privation, possession, or something else, the syllogism would be formed. So the cold; indeed the truth (J) had two relations to 'A': one that it is not attributed to it, and the other that it is the opposite of what is attributed to it. But the syllogism is formed from it only because it is not attributed. So if you preserve that it is not attributed and you replace its being opposite to what is attributed, the desired syllogism continues. And this applies to everything of this kind; thus the reverse.

Commentary
This passage provides a practical algorithm for constructing a categorical syllogism in any of the four figures by examining the 'lawāḥiq' (attributes) of the subject and the 'malḥūqāt' (things attributed to) the predicate of the desired conclusion. The author first enumerates all possible cases for a universal affirmative conclusion: if you find an attribute of the subject that is also affirmed of the predicate, you get the first figure. For a universal negative: if that attribute is denied of the predicate, again the first figure. For the second figure: you find an attribute of one extreme that is denied of every individual of the other extreme via a common predicate. For the fourth figure: an attribute of every predicate individual that is denied of the subject. For particular affirmatives: similar but with existential quantification. The author then explains a method to efficiently test for the middle term by starting with the most general attribute: if it is not an attribute, then all subsumed ones also are not; if it is an attribute, descend stepwise to find the right level. He gives an example: 'the negation of rational from whiteness' is not direct but via body or substance. A critical point is that one must not confuse opposition with mere non-attribution; the middle term must be a single thing. Opposites would require two syllogisms. The key is that the negation is due to non-attribution, not contradiction. Therefore, when seeking a negative conclusion, you should look for a middle that is simply not attributed, not one that is opposite. The passage concludes by stating that this rule applies analogously to other cases.

Identifying the Third and Fourth Figures of Syllogism
Definition of third and fourth figures based on middle term position and premise quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a categorical syllogism is in the third or fourth figure by analyzing the position of the middle term and the quality of the premises relative to the conclusion.

Core Idea
Figures are defined by the middle term's relation to the conclusion's subject and predicate.

This passage defines the third and fourth figures of the categorical syllogism. These two figures are essential for classifying arguments in Islamic logic. The author provides conditions based on affirmation (ījāb) and negation (salab) and clarifies that 'subject' and 'predicate' refer to the conclusion. Let's break down these definitions step by step so you can recognize these figures in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate of the conclusion
The author explicitly states that 'subject' and 'predicate' in the definition refer to the subject and predicate of the desired conclusion (al-maṭlūb). So first determine what the conclusion asserts.
The conclusion is the statement you are trying to prove.
2
Locate the middle term in the premises
For the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises. For the fourth figure, the middle term is the predicate of the major premise and the subject of the minor premise.
The major premise contains the predicate of the conclusion; the minor contains the subject.
3
Check the quality of the premises
Third figure: one premise is affirmative and the other is negative regarding the predicate (i.e., one affirms something of the subject, the other negates something of the predicate). Fourth figure: the minor premise is affirmative (predicating something of all or some of the subject), and the major premise is a universal negative (negating something from all individuals of the predicate).
Affirmation and negation here refer to the connection with the middle term.
4
Apply the definition to confirm the figure
If the middle term is subject in both premises and the premises have one affirmation and one negation, it is the third figure. If the middle term is predicate in the major and subject in the minor, with an affirmative minor and universal negative major, it is the fourth figure.
The fourth figure always has a universal negative major premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالإيجاب ما يسلب عن جميع آحاد الموضوع أو عن بعض آحاد محموله أو وجدت في ملحوقات البعض من آحاد الموضوع بالإيجاب ما يسلب عن جميع آحاد المحمول فقد انعقد من الشكل الثالث. وإن وجدت ما يحمل على كل واحد من آحاد الموضوع أو على البعض منها بالإيجاب ويسلب عن كل واحد من آحاد المحمول فقد انعقد من الشكل الرابع. والمراد من الموضوع والمحمول فيما ذكرناه موضوع المطلوب ومحموله.
By affirmation, what is negated from all individuals of the subject or from some individuals of its predicate, or found in the followers of some individuals of the subject by affirmation, what is negated from all individuals of the predicate, then the third figure is formed. And if you find something that is predicated of every individual of the subject or of some of them by affirmation, and is negated from every individual of the predicate, then the fourth figure is formed. By subject and predicate in what we mentioned is meant the subject of the desired conclusion and its predicate.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the third and fourth figures of the categorical syllogism. The author describes the conditions under which each figure is formed based on the relations of affirmation (ījāb) and negation (salab) between the subject and predicate of the conclusion and the middle term. In the third figure, the middle term is subject of both premises, and one premise is affirmative while the other is negative with respect to the predicate. In the fourth figure, the middle term is predicate of the major premise and subject of the minor, with a specific combination of affirmation and negation. The author then clarifies that 'subject' and 'predicate' here refer to the subject and predicate of the desired conclusion (al-maṭlūb). This understanding is crucial for correctly identifying the figure of any given syllogism.

Key Takeaway
