The Syllogism
beginner
6 min
Identifying Premises in a Categorical Syllogism
Explains how to identify minor and major premises in categorical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor and major premises in any categorical syllogism by locating the term shared with the conclusion.

Core Idea
The shared term tells you which premise is minor or major.

In this passage, the author teaches us how to determine the roles of premises in a categorical syllogism. By checking whether the term shared with the conclusion is the subject or predicate, we immediately know which premise is minor and which is major. This skill is essential for evaluating the validity of arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the shared term
Look at the conclusion (al-matlab). Identify which term from the conclusion appears in both premises. That is the shared limit (hadd).
In logical analysis, the conclusion has two parts: subject (mawdu‘) and predicate (mahmul).
2
Classify the premise
If the shared term is the subject of the conclusion, then that premise is the minor (sughra). If it is the predicate, then that premise is the major (kubra).
This classification is the first step in reconstructing a syllogism.
3
Identify the middle term
The other term in the premise (after removing the shared term) will become the middle term (hadd al-awsat) if the syllogism is simple. It must relate to the other part of the conclusion through one of the four figures.
The middle term connects the subject and predicate of the conclusion.
4
Verify the figure
Check whether the middle term appears in the remaining positions (subject or predicate of each premise) to fall into one of the four figures. If it does, the syllogism is simple (qiyas iqtirani). If not, it is composite.
The four figures are standard in categorical logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كانت المشاركة بينهما في حد واحد من حدي المطلوب لا غير كان القياس اقترانيا. ثم انظر أن ذلك الحد هو موضوع المطلوب أو محموله؛ فإن كان موضوع المطلوب فتلك المقدمة هي الصغرى، وإن كان محموله فتلك المقدّمة هي الكبرى. وعلى كل واحد من التقديرين فلا بد من أن ينسب الجزء الآخر من تلك المقدمة إلى الجزء الآخر من المطلوب على أحد أنحاء التأليفات الأربعة التي هي بحسب الأشكال الأربعة : فإن تألف فالجزء الآخر هو الوسط، وتلك المقدّمة أعني المؤلفة منهما هي الكبرى إن كانت الأولى هي الصغرى، أو الصغرى إن كانت الأولى هي الكبرى؛ وحينئذ تميزت لك المقدمتان بالفعل، وشكل القياس والنتيجة. وإن لم يتألف أحد أنحاء التأليفات الأربعة أصلا كان القياس مركباً لا بسيطا؛ وحينئذ لا بد من مقدمة أخرى تشارك الجزء الآخر من المطلوب. وكان بينها وبين المقدمة الأولى مقدّمات أخرى؛ فألف بين كل مقدمتين تجد بينهما اشتراكا، وتدرج منه إلى نتيجة ينتجه إلى أن يصل إلى القياس القريب المنتج للمطلوب. وإن لم تجد بين المقدمتين من المقدمات الواقعة بينهما اشتراكًا فهناك شيء مضمر لا محالة، وتحتاج إلى استخراج وسط جامع بينهما مثلا إذا كان المطلوب موجبا كليا وهو قولنا كل «أط» ؛ وقد وجدنا كل «أب» وكل «ج د» وكل «ج ط» فقد وجدنا مقدمتين كل واحدة منهما تشارك المطلوب في أحد جزأيه لكن ليس بين هذه المقدمات الثلاث اشتراك؛ وحينئذ إن حصل لنا وسط نجمع بين «ب» و «ج»؛ ووسط آخر نجمع بين «د» و «ه»، فقد تم القياس بالفعل وإلا فلا. وأما القياس المركب فأقل حدوده أن تكون أربعة وليكن المطلوب موجبا كليا وهو أن كل (ج) أ ) ؛ وليكن معنا من المقدمات كل «ج ب» وكل «و»، «أ». فإن كان بينا إن كان كل ب د انتظم القياس المنتج للمطلوب وإلا فنحتاج إلى وسط نجمع بين «ب» و «د». وليكن المطلوب سالبا كليا وهو لا شيء من ج (أ) ومعنا من المقدمات كل (ج) ب) ولا شيء من «د أه؛ فإن كان بينا إن كان كل «ب د» فقد انعقد القياس المنتج لذلك المطلوب وإلا فنحتاج إلى وسط.
And if the sharing between them is in one limit only of the two limits of the sought conclusion, then the syllogism is categorical (iqtiṛānī). Then consider: is that limit the subject of the sought conclusion or its predicate? If it is the subject of the sought conclusion, then that premise is the minor; and if it is its predicate, then that premise is the major. And in each of the two cases, it is necessary that the other part of that premise be related to the other part of the sought conclusion in one of the four modes of composition which are according to the four figures. So if it is composed, then the other part is the middle term, and that premise — I mean the one composed from them — is the major if the first was the minor, or the minor if the first was the major; and thereupon the two premises become distinguished actually, and the figure of the syllogism and the conclusion. And if none of the four modes of composition is composed at all, then the syllogism is composite (murakkab), not simple. And then it is necessary that there be another premise that shares the other part of the sought conclusion. And between it and the first premise there are other premises. So compose between every two premises you find a sharing between them, and progress gradually from it to a conclusion that it produces until you reach the immediate syllogism that produces the sought conclusion. And if you do not find between the two premises among the premises that lie between them a sharing, then there is something implicit without doubt, and you need to extract a middle term that unites them. For example, if the sought conclusion is universal affirmative, which is our statement 'every A is T', and we have found 'every A is B' and 'every J is D' and 'every J is T', then we have found two premises each one of which shares the sought conclusion in one of its two parts, but there is no sharing among these three premises. Then if we obtain a middle term that unites B and J, and another middle term that unites D and H, then the syllogism is actually completed, otherwise not. And as for the composite syllogism, its minimum number of premises is four. Let the sought conclusion be universal affirmative, that every J is A, and let us have from premises 'every J is B' and 'every W is A'. Then if it is evident that 'every B is D', the syllogism that produces the sought conclusion is arranged; otherwise we need a middle term that unites B and D. And let the sought conclusion be universal negative, that no J is A, and we have from premises 'every J is B' and 'no D is A'. Then if it is evident that 'every B is D', the syllogism that produces that sought conclusion is concluded; otherwise we need a middle term.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage continues the method for constructing syllogisms when the sharing between premises is only in one term of the conclusion. This is the case of the categorical (iqtiṛānī) syllogism. The author tells us to identify whether that shared term is the subject or predicate of the conclusion. If it is the subject, the premise containing it is the minor premise; if predicate, it is the major. Then we must check how the other term of that premise relates to the other term of the conclusion in one of the four figures (the four modes of composition). If a valid figure is found, the other term becomes the middle term, and we can identify the shape and conclusion. If no direct composition works, the syllogism is composite (murakkab): we need additional premises to connect the terms. The author gives examples using variables: for a universal affirmative conclusion 'every A is T', we have premises 'every A is B', 'every J is D', 'every J is T'. These don't share directly, so we need middle terms to link B with J and D with H (or similar). The composite syllogism minimally requires four premises. For a universal negative conclusion 'no J is A', with premises 'every J is B' and 'no D is A', if we can link B and D via 'every B is D', we get a valid syllogism; otherwise we need a middle term. This systematic approach teaches the logician how to build valid arguments from given premises by identifying figures and, when necessary, chaining syllogisms.

Key Terms
قياس اقتراني
صغرى
كبرى
وسط

Key Takeaway
In a categorical syllogism, the shared term between premises and conclusion determines which premise is minor (if subject) or major (if predicate).

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Recognizing Composite Syllogisms and Chaining Premises
Explains when a syllogism becomes composite and how to chain premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a syllogism is composite (requiring more than two premises) and identify the intermediate middle terms needed to connect them.

Core Idea
If no single figure works, you need a chain of syllogisms.

Sometimes the premises don't directly connect in a single syllogism. The author introduces the concept of a composite syllogism (qiyas murakkab) which requires additional premises to bridge the gap. By learning to detect when no direct composition is possible and to insert middle terms, you can construct complex arguments from given data.

Guided Path
1
Check direct composition
Take the two premises that each share one term with the conclusion. Try to relate the remaining terms through the four figures. If no figure works, the syllogism is composite.
The four figures are the standard ways to arrange the middle term.
2
Count the premises
A composite syllogism requires at least four premises. Each additional premise serves to connect terms that are not directly linked.
The author states 'its minimum number of premises is four'.
3
Insert intermediate middle terms
Find missing connections between the terms. For example, if you have 'A is B' and 'J is T' but no direct link, you may need premises like 'Every B is D' and 'Every D is J' to chain them.
The author gives an example with variables: to connect B and J, you need a middle term uniting them.
4
Build the chain
Compose syllogisms step by step: from the given premises, derive intermediate conclusions that become premises for the next syllogism, until the final conclusion is reached.
This is method of progression (tadarruj) mentioned in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Parsing the Construction of Compound Syllogisms
Author explains how to find middle terms and build compound syllogisms for A, E, I, O conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to follow the author's case-by-case method for constructing compound syllogisms for all four categorical propositions.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms require at least four terms; the author shows how to add middle terms step by step.

This passage is a detailed manual for building compound syllogisms. We will break down the author’s instructions for each type of conclusion, focusing on how to identify missing middle terms and how to arrange premises into valid figures.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general condition
If two premises do not compose in any of the four figures, you need a compound syllogism. The passage begins: 'If it does not compose... the syllogism is compound.'
2
Work through the universal affirmative example
The author gives conclusion 'every A is T' with premises 'every A is B', 'every J is D', 'every J is T'. Notice there is no single middle term linking A and T via the premises. He then says you need two middle terms: one linking B and J, another linking D and H.
The variables are arbitrary; in Islamic sciences you would replace them with real terms.
3
Apply the universal negative case with figure restriction
For conclusion 'nothing of J is A', premises 'every J is B' and 'nothing of D is A'. If we also have 'every B is D', we can form the syllogism. The author warns that if the minor premise is negative, it cannot be used in the first figure.
This restriction is crucial for valid reasoning.
4
Examine the particular affirmative example
For conclusion 'some J is A', premises 'some J is D' and 'every B is A'. The author shows that if we obtain 'every J is B' or 'every D is B', we can form the compound syllogism.
Notice the use of the third figure to generate an intermediate conclusion.
5
Trace the particular negative example
For conclusion 'not some J is A', premises 'some J is B' and 'nothing of D is A'. If we add 'every D is B', we form a third-figure syllogism to get some J is B, then combine with a second-figure syllogism.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

من «د أ»؛ فإن كان بينا أن كل «ب د» فقد انعقد القياس المنتج لذلك المطلوب وإلا فنحتاج إلى وسط نجمع بين «ب» و«د». وليكن الموجود معنا من المقدمات لا شيء من «ج ب» وكل «أ د» لا ينفعنا هنا أن نجد كل «ب د» لامتناع أن تكون السالبة هي الصغرى في الشكل الأول. نعم لو صح لنا كل «ج ب» فنقول كل «ج ب» ولا شيء من «د ج» ينتج لا شيء من «ج د»؛ ثم نضيف إليه كل «أ د» ينتج لا شيء من «ج د»؛ فكذلك إذا كان الموجود معنا لا شيء من «ج ب» وكل «د أ» فلا ننتفع بهما. وليكن المطلوب موجبا جزئيا وهو بعض «ج أ» والموجود معنا بعض «ج د» وكل «ب أ»؛ فإن حصل لنا كل «د ب» فقد وجدنا القياس المنتج للمطلوب. وإن كان الموجود معنا بعض «ج د» وكل «ب أ»؛ فإن حصل لنا كل «ج ب» فقد وجدنا القياس أيضًا. وإن كان الموجود معنا كل «د ج» وكل «ب أ»؛ فإن صدق كل «ج ب» فقد وجد المطلوب أيضًا لأنا نؤلف قياسا في الثالث من قولنا كل «ج د» وكل «د ب» لينتج قولنا بعض «د ب»، ثم نضم إليه كل «ب أ» حتى ينتج المطلوب من الشكل الأول. وليكن المطلوب سالبا جزئيا وهو قولنا ليس بعض «ج أ» والموجود معنا بعض «ج ب» ولا شيء من «د أ»؛ فقد وجد القياس المنتج للمطلوب. وكذلك إذا كان الموجود بعض «د ج» ولا شيء من «أ ب» وقد حصل لنا كل «د ب»؛ فإن القياس ينعقد من الثالث حينئذ كقولنا بعض «د ج» وكل «د ب» فإنه ينتج بعض «ج ب». وينتظم مع قولنا لا شيء من «أ ب» قياس من الثاني؛ وينتج المطلوب. وإن كان الموجود ليس كل «ج ب» وكل «أ ج» وقد حصل لنا كل «ب د» فالقياس ينعقد من الثاني كقولنا ليس كل «ج ب» وكل «د ب» ينتج ليس كل «ج د» وينعقد من هذه النتيجة ومن قولنا كل «أ د» قياس من الثاني أيضًا منتج للمطلوب. وإن كان الموجود بعض «ج ب» ولا شيء من «أ د» واتصل به كل «ج د» فالقياس ينعقد من الأول؛ وينتج كقولنا بعض «ج ب» وكل «ب د» ينتج بعض «ج د».
Its product: that premise is the major. In each of the two cases, it is necessary that the other part of that premise be related to the other part of the desired conclusion in one of the four types of composition which correspond to the four figures. If it does compose, then the other part is the middle term, and that premise—I mean the one composed of them—is the major if the first was the minor, or the minor if the first was the major. Then the two premises become actually distinct, along with the figure of the syllogism and the conclusion. If none of the four types of composition occurs at all, then the syllogism is compound, not simple. In that case, another premise is needed that shares the other part of the desired conclusion. Between it and the first premise there are other premises. So compose between every two premises you find sharing a term, and proceed from that to a result that leads you until you reach the proximate syllogism that yields the desired conclusion. If you do not find between the two premises—among the premises situated between them—a shared term, then there is necessarily an implicit thing, and you need to extract a middle term that unites them. For example, if the desired conclusion is a universal affirmative, namely our statement 'Every A is T,' and we have found 'Every A is B' and 'Every J is D' and 'Every J is T,' then we have found two premises each of which shares with the desired conclusion in one of its parts, but among these three premises there is no common term. Then if we obtain a middle term that unites B and J, and another middle that unites D and H, then the syllogism is actually completed; otherwise not. As for the compound syllogism, its minimum is four. Let the desired conclusion be a universal affirmative, namely that every J is A, and we have from the premises 'Every J is B' and 'Every W is A.' Then if it is evident that every B is D, the syllogism producing the desired conclusion is formed; otherwise we need a middle term that unites B and D. Let the desired conclusion be a universal negative, namely nothing of J is A, and we have from the premises 'Every J is B' and 'Nothing of D is A.' Then if it is evident that every B is D, the syllogism producing that desired conclusion is formed; otherwise we need a middle term that unites B and D. Let the premises we have be 'Nothing of J is B' and 'Every A is D'—finding 'Every B is D' does not benefit us here because it is impossible for the negative to be the minor in the first figure. Rather, if we had 'Every J is B' we would say 'Every J is B' and 'Nothing of D is J' yields 'Nothing of J is D'; then we add to it 'Every A is D' yields 'Nothing of J is D'. Similarly, if we have 'Nothing of J is B' and 'Every D is A', we cannot benefit from them. Let the desired conclusion be a particular affirmative, namely some J is A, and we have some J is D and every B is A. Then if we obtain 'Every D is B', we have found the syllogism yielding the desired conclusion. If we have some J is D and every B is A, then if we obtain 'Every J is B', we have also found the syllogism. If we have 'Every D is J' and every B is A, then if 'Every J is B' is true, we have also found the desired conclusion, because we form a syllogism in the third figure from our statements 'Every J is D' and 'Every D is B' to yield some D is B, then we join to it 'Every B is A' so that the desired conclusion is produced from the first figure. Let the desired conclusion be a particular negative, namely our statement 'Not some J is A', and we have some J is B and nothing of D is A; then the syllogism yielding the desired conclusion is found. Also, if we have some D is J and nothing of A is B, and we have obtained 'Every D is B', then the syllogism is formed in the third figure in that case, like our statement 'Some D is J' and 'Every D is B' yields some J is B. It combines with our statement 'Nothing of A is B' to form a syllogism in the second figure, yielding the desired conclusion. If we have 'Not every J is B' and 'Every A is J', and we have obtained 'Every B is D', then the syllogism is formed in the second figure, like our statement 'Not every J is B' and 'Every D is B' yields 'Not every J is D', and from this result and our statement 'Every A is D' a syllogism in the second figure is also formed, producing the desired conclusion. If we have some J is B and nothing of A is D, and connected to it is 'Every J is D', then the syllogism is formed in the first figure, and it yields, like our statement 'Some J is B' and 'Every B is D' yields 'Some J is D'.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a detailed, case-by-case examination of how to construct compound syllogisms when the given premises do not immediately combine to yield the desired conclusion. The author first explains the general method: if the two premises do not share a middle term in one of the four figures, we need additional premises. We then look for common terms to link premises step by step until we reach a proximate syllogism that directly produces the conclusion. The first example illustrates this with a universal affirmative conclusion (Every A is T) and three premises, showing that we need two middle terms to connect them. The author then turns to compound syllogisms proper, stating that they require at least four terms (i.e., three premises). He proceeds to run through each of the four categorical proposition types—universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative—showing in each case how to determine whether the given premises are sufficient or whether an additional middle term is needed. A key point is the restriction on the first figure: the minor premise cannot be negative. This is highlighted in the universal negative case where the premises 'Nothing of J is B' and 'Every A is D' cannot be used directly because the negative would be the minor. The author then shows an alternative route using an intermediate syllogism. The commentary follows the author's own order, explaining each case as it appears. The student should pay close attention to the logical variables and how they shift roles across figures.

Syllogisms Without Conversion via Shared Limit
Author explains conditions for direct syllogistic inference without conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a categorical syllogism can be formed without conversion by checking whether a premise shares the limit (hadd) of the conclusion.

Core Idea
Sharing the limit eliminates the need for conversion, simplifying syllogistic analysis.

In this passage, the author explains that if a premise shares a term (limit) with the conclusion, a valid syllogism can often be produced directly without converting any proposition. Understanding this principle allows you to quickly identify when a conclusion follows from given premises without extra steps.

Guided Path
1
Identify the limit (hadd) of the conclusion
Look at the conclusion: it has a subject and predicate. The limit is the term that is shared with a premise. For example, if the conclusion is 'some J is D', the terms are 'J' and 'D'. If a premise contains 'J' or 'D', that term is a shared limit.
2
Check if both premises share the limit
If each premise contains the same limit term from the conclusion, then a direct syllogism from the productive figures (first or second) yields the conclusion without conversion. For instance, from 'some J is B' and 'every B is D', both share 'D' indirectly? No, actually check: both share J? The author means each premise shares at least one limit term. Follow his examples.
The author says 'if the premises share the limit' meaning each premise shares one of the limit terms.
3
If only one premise shares, examine how
When only one premise contains a limit term, determine whether that term is the subject or predicate of the conclusion. The author provides two cases: sharing in subject (e.g., the premise has the subject term) or sharing in predicate.
4
Apply the rule for subject-sharing
If the shared limit is the subject of the conclusion, then you can often form a first-figure syllogism directly. For example, if the conclusion is 'every J is A' and you have 'every J is B' and 'every B is D', then if 'A' connects to 'D' you obtain the result without conversion.
This is a simplified rule; the author gives more details for universal and particular forms.
5
Apply the rule for predicate-sharing
If the shared limit is the predicate of the conclusion, then you also form a first-figure syllogism, but with the predicate as the middle term. For example, conclusion 'every J is A', premises 'every D is B' and 'every B is A', and 'every J is D' yields the conclusion without conversion.
Again, the author covers universal and particular cases.

He emphasizes that in these cases, no conversion is needed, and concludes that this suffices after understanding compositions and their results.

Prioritizing Meaning over Wording in Syllogistic Analysis
Author advises focusing on meaning not wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify cases of synonymy or compound terms in syllogistic premises and apply the method of replacing compound expressions with single terms to clarify logical structure.

Core Idea
Meaning is the true objective; ignore verbal differences when meaning agrees.

In logic, we often encounter terms that are not exactly the same in wording across premises and conclusion. The author warns us not to be confused by such differences. Instead, we must focus on the meaning and replace compound expressions with single-word equivalents. This lesson teaches you how to handle synonymy and compound terms to keep your syllogistic analysis clean.

Guided Path
1
Identify word differences
Look at all occurrences of the same term across premises and conclusion. Note any differences in wording: is the term expressed differently, perhaps as a synonym or a compound phrase?
The author says: 'perhaps the wording in the conclusion is different from the wording in the premise.'
2
Determine if meaning changes
Check whether the different wordings refer to the same meaning. If they are synonyms, the logical relationship is unaffected. If they are compound vs. single, the meaning may still be the same.
The author emphasizes: 'the intended goal is meaning in reality.'
3
Replace compound with single term
If a term is compound (e.g., a descriptive phrase), replace it with a single word that captures its essence. This ensures uniformity in the syllogistic analysis.
For example, 'not odd' could be replaced by 'even' if they are equivalent.
4
Verify the logical structure
After replacement, check that the syllogism still follows the correct figure and mood. The terms should now be clearly related.
If the meaning is preserved, the validity should be unaffected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And perhaps the wording in the conclusion is different from the wording in the premise, or in one of the two premises is different from the other; then the necessary [action] is to occupy oneself with meaning and not with difference of wording when meaning agrees; for the intended goal is meaning in reality. And perhaps the terms do not have single words but are compound, and perhaps in one premise it is single and in the other compound; so analysis should not confuse you due to this difference, rather you must replace the compound with the single and not neglect observance of negation (adl) and simple negation (salb). For perhaps the conclusion is affirmative while the middle term is accompanied by the letter of negation in both premises; so you wonder at the conclusion being affirmative. And it is only like that because the middle term is negated (maddul), as in our statement: 'This number is not odd, and every number is not odd, therefore it is even; so this number is even.' And as for the difference between adl and salb, it has passed before.

Commentary
This passage gives practical advice for analyzing syllogisms when there is synonymy or compound terms. The author emphasizes that meaning is the true objective; differences in wording that do not affect meaning should be ignored. If a term is compound, it should be replaced by a single-word equivalent. Then he discusses a counterintuitive case: when the middle term is negated (adl) in both premises, the conclusion can be affirmative. He provides an example: 'This number is not odd' and 'Every number is not odd' leads to 'This number is even.' The key is that 'not odd' is a negated predicate equivalent to 'even'. He reminds that the distinction between adl (negation of predicate) and salb (negation of copula) has been covered earlier.

The Surprising Affirmative Conclusion from a Negated Middle Term
Author explains the effect of negated middle terms on conclusion quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a syllogism with a negated middle term in both premises can yield an affirmative conclusion, using the concept of adl (negated predicate).

Core Idea
When the middle term is negated by adl, it can be equivalent to a positive term, allowing an affirmative conclusion.

The author presents a counterintuitive case: the conclusion is affirmative even though the middle term is accompanied by the letter of negation in both premises. This happens when the negation is adl (negation of the predicate itself), turning the terms into positive equivalents. This lesson shows you how to recognize adl and handle such cases.

Guided Path
1
Locate the middle term
In any given syllogism, find the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. In the example, the middle term is 'number', but the key is the predicate 'not odd'.
2
Check for negation signs
See if the middle term is accompanied by a negation word (e.g., 'not') in both premises. In the example, both premises contain 'not odd'.
3
Distinguish adl from salb
Determine if the negation is adl (negation of the predicate itself) or salb (negation of the copula). In the example, 'not odd' is a negated predicate (adl) because it modifies the term directly, not the copula. The author says: 'the middle term is negated (maddul)'.
Adl makes the predicate term negative in itself; salb would negate the copula (e.g., 'is not').
4
Replace the negated predicate with its positive equivalent
If the negation is adl, the negated term can be replaced by a positive term that has the same meaning. In the example, 'not odd' is equivalent to 'even'.
5
Re-evaluate the syllogism with positive terms
After replacement, the syllogism becomes: 'Every number is even, this number is even, therefore this number is even.' This is a valid first-figure syllogism (Barbara) with an affirmative conclusion.
The original conclusion was already affirmative, but now we see why.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربَّما كان اللفظ في النتيجة غير اللفظ في المقدمة أو كان في إحدى المقدمتين غير ما في الأخرى؛ واللازم حينئذ هو الاشتغال بالمعنى لا إلى اختلاف اللفظ عند اتفاق المعنى؛ إذ المقصود هو المعنى بالحقيقة. وربَّما لم تكن للحدود ألفاظا مفردة بل مركبة، وربَّما كان في إحدى المقدمتين مفرد وفي الأخرى مركب؛ فلا ينبغي أن يتغوش عليك التحليل بسبب هذا الاختلاف بل عليك أن تُبدل المركَّب بالمفرد ولا تذهل عن مراعاة العدول والسلب، فربَّما كانت النتيجة موجبة والوسط مقرون حرف السلب في المقدمتين جميعًا؛ فتتعجب من كون النتيجة موجبة. وإنما كان كذلك لكون الوسط معدولا كما في قولنا هذا العدد هو لا فرد وكل عدد هو لا فرد فهو زوج فهذا العدد زوج، وأما الفرق بين العدول والسلب فقد مرَّ من قبل.
And perhaps the wording in the conclusion is different from the wording in the premise, or in one of the two premises is different from the other; then the necessary [action] is to occupy oneself with meaning and not with difference of wording when meaning agrees; for the intended goal is meaning in reality. And perhaps the terms do not have single words but are compound, and perhaps in one premise it is single and in the other compound; so analysis should not confuse you due to this difference, rather you must replace the compound with the single and not neglect observance of negation (adl) and simple negation (salb). For perhaps the conclusion is affirmative while the middle term is accompanied by the letter of negation in both premises; so you wonder at the conclusion being affirmative. And it is only like that because the middle term is negated (maddul), as in our statement: 'This number is not odd, and every number is not odd, therefore it is even; so this number is even.' And as for the difference between adl and salb, it has passed before.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage gives practical advice for analyzing syllogisms when there is synonymy or compound terms. The author emphasizes that meaning is the true objective; differences in wording that do not affect meaning should be ignored. If a term is compound, it should be replaced by a single-word equivalent. Then he discusses a counterintuitive case: when the middle term is negated (adl) in both premises, the conclusion can be affirmative. He provides an example: 'This number is not odd' and 'Every number is not odd' leads to 'This number is even.' The key is that 'not odd' is a negated predicate equivalent to 'even'. He reminds that the distinction between adl (negation of predicate) and salb (negation of copula) has been covered earlier.

سلب

D) Because the middle term is not actually negated

Key Takeaway
A negated middle term can lead to an affirmative conclusion when the negation is adl (negated predicate) rather than salb (simple negation of the copula).

The Syllogism

Identifying and Completing Incomplete Syllogisms
Author discusses incomplete syllogisms that appear complete

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an incomplete syllogism that appears to give the conclusion directly and supply the missing premise to reconstruct a valid categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
An incomplete syllogism tricks the mind into thinking the conclusion follows without a missing premise.

Some syllogisms appear to give the conclusion immediately, causing the mind to think they are complete. The author illustrates this with an example about substance: the parts of substance being annihilated annihilates substance; what is not substance does not. The mind concludes that parts are substance, but a universal premise is missing. This lesson will train you to spot such incomplete syllogisms and reduce them to their full form.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the apparent entailment
Read the example: 'The parts of substance, by their being annihilated, the substance is annihilated; and what is not substance is not annihilated by its annihilation the substance. Therefore, the parts of substance are substance.' Notice how the conclusion seems to follow directly from the first statement alone.
2
Identify the missing general premise
The conclusion only follows if we add a universal statement that connects the subject to the predicate. What general rule would make the argument valid? The missing premise is: 'Whatever, when annihilated, annihilates substance is itself substance.'
This premise links 'parts of substance' (minor term) to 'substance' (major term) via the middle term 'annihilating substance by annihilation'.
3
Reconstruct the complete syllogism
Supply the missing premise as the major premise: 'Whatever annihilates substance by its annihilation is substance.' The minor premise: 'The parts of substance annihilate substance by their annihilation.' Then the conclusion: 'Therefore, the parts of substance are substance.' This forms a Barbara syllogism (AAA-1).
4
Verify validity
Check that the middle term 'annihilating substance by annihilation' is distributed in the major premise and appears in the minor premise. The syllogism now satisfies the rules of the first figure: the conclusion necessarily follows. The mind's perplexity dissolves once the missing premise is explicitly stated.
The original incomplete form caused confusion because the missing premise was implicit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في ذكر التأليفات القياسية التي يعسر تحليلها وذكر ما يسهل به ذلك. وأما ذكر التأليفات القياسية التي يعسر تحليلها وذكر ما يسهل به ذلك؛ فذلك من اللوازم أنه كثيرًا يعسر تحليل القياس الناقص إذا كان ظاهر المسموع منه تلزم عنه النتيجة عند الذهن فيظنه تامًا ويمتنع عن رده إلى كماله. ثم لا نجد العدد الموجود مشاركًا للنتيجة مشاركة ينبغي أن يكون بينها وبين المقدمات مثاله أن أجزاء الجوهر تبطل ببطلانها الجوهر، وما ليس بجوهر لا يبطل ببطلانه الجوهر. أو نقول بطلان أجزاء الجوهر يبطل معه الجوهر، وبطلان ما ليس بجوهر لا يبطل معه الجوهر، فيلزم منه أن أجزاء الجوهر جوهر ثمَّ الذهن لما اعتقد أنه تام يلزمه هذا فكان يعتقد أنه لا يمكن تحليله ورفعه إلى ما يلزم منه المطلوب فيبلد فيه. وربَّما كان المطلوب في مثل ذلك لازما للنتيجة القريبة من القياس لزوم التالي للمقدم كما مرَّ بالنسبة إلى العبارة الأولى.
Chapter: On Mentioning the Syllogistic Compositions That Are Difficult to Analyze and Mentioning What Makes That Easy. As for mentioning the syllogistic compositions that are difficult to analyze and what makes that easy, that is among the necessary [things] because often the analysis of an incomplete syllogism is difficult when the apparent heard [statement] from it entails the conclusion in the mind, so [the mind] thinks it is complete and refrains from reducing it to its perfection. Then we do not find the existing number sharing with the conclusion a sharing that should be between it and the premises. Its example: that the parts of substance, by their being annihilated, the substance is annihilated; and what is not substance is not annihilated by its annihilation the substance. Or we say: the annihilation of the parts of substance annihilates the substance along with it, and the annihilation of what is not substance does not annihilate substance along with it. So it follows that the parts of substance are substance. Then the mind, when it believed it is complete, this follows from it, so it believed that it cannot be analyzed and raised to what produces the desired, so it is perplexed in it. And perhaps the desired in such is a necessary consequent of the near conclusion of the syllogism, like the entailment of the consequent from the antecedent, as has passed with respect to the first expression.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This chapter addresses a special type of syllogism: the incomplete syllogism (qiyas naqis) that appears to give the conclusion directly, leading the mind to think it is complete. The author gives an example: 'The parts of substance are such that when they are annihilated, substance is annihilated. What is not substance does not annihilate substance when annihilated. Therefore, the parts of substance are substance.' The mind sees this as a valid conclusion but the syllogism is missing a premise (e.g., 'Whatever annihilates substance by its annihilation is substance'). The author notes that the desired conclusion may be an immediate consequent of the near conclusion, causing confusion. The remedy is to recognize the missing premise and reduce the syllogism to a full form.

Completing Incomplete Syllogisms: The Substance Example
Author illustrates incomplete syllogism with substance example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an incomplete syllogism and complete it by adding a missing premise using conversion or contraposition.

Core Idea
An incomplete syllogism hides a missing premise that you must make explicit.

In this lesson, we examine a classic example from the source: the argument that parts of substance are substance. The premises appear to yield the conclusion directly, but a step is missing. We will learn how to spot such incomplete syllogisms and fill in the gap using logical transformations like conversion and contraposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the given premises and conclusion
Look at the substance example: Premise 1: 'Parts of substance corrupt substance.' Premise 2: 'What is not substance does not corrupt substance.' Conclusion: 'Parts of substance are substance.'
2
Detect the missing link
The conclusion cannot be directly inferred from the two premises. The missing premise is a general statement connecting 'corrupts substance' to 'is substance'.
Think: what property links the subject and predicate?
3
Use conversion or contraposition to derive the missing premise
Convert the second premise 'What is not substance does not corrupt substance' via contrapositive: 'What corrupts substance is substance.' This becomes the major premise.
Contrapositive: 'If not B then not A' becomes 'If A then B.'
4
Reconstruct the complete syllogism
Now we have a Barbara syllogism: Major: Whatever corrupts substance is substance. Minor: Parts of substance corrupt substance. Conclusion: Parts of substance are substance. This is valid.
Check that the middle term 'corrupts substance' appears in both premises.

فصل : [ في ذكر التأليفات القياسية التي يعسر تحليلها وذكر ما يسهل به ذلك ] وأما ذكر التأليفات القياسية التي يعسر تحليلها وذكر ما يسهل به ذلك؛ فذلك من اللوازم أنه كثيرًا يعسر تحليل القياس الناقص إذا كان الجوهر، وما ليس بجوهر لا يبطل ببطلانه الجوهر. أو نقول بطلان أجزاء الجوهر يبطل معه الجوهر، وبطلان ما ليس بجوهر لا يبطل معه الجوهر، فيلزم منه أن أجزاء الجوهر جوهر ثمَّ الذهن لما اعتقد أنه تام يلزمه هذا وإن كان الحيوان موجودا فالجوهر موجود فينتج إن كان الإنسان موجودا فالجوهر موجود. وقد قيل فيه: «إنه من جملة ما يعسر تحليله لما أنه يتألف من شرطيتين». ومع ذلك فليس فيه استثناء فليس بقياس، وقيل: «إنه يعسر كما أنه من المهملتين. وينبغي أن يكون ما يقال في مثل هذا الحفظ أشياء أحدها أن يكون المطلوب حقًا، والثاني أن يكون لازما غير ذلك القول، والثالث أنه ليس ينتج ذلك؛ إما لأنه غير قياس وإما لأنه قياس، وينتج Section: On the mention of syllogistic compositions that are difficult to analyze and mention of what facilitates that. As for the mention of syllogistic compositions that are difficult to analyze and mention of what facilitates that, that is among the necessities: often the analysis of an incomplete syllogism is difficult when the apparent heard [statement] from it entails the conclusion in the mind, so he thinks it is complete and refrains from reducing it to its completion. Then we do not find the existing number participating in the conclusion in a participation that should be between it and the premises. An example of that: parts of substance: their corruption corrupts the substance, and what is not a substance, its corruption does not corrupt the substance. Or we say: corruption of parts of substance corrupts substance with it, and corruption of what is not substance does not corrupt substance with it, so it follows that parts of substance are substance. Then the mind, when it believes it is complete, this follows, so he thinks it cannot be analyzed and raised to what entails the desired [conclusion], so he is confused in it. And perhaps the desired [conclusion] in such [cases] is a necessary consequent of the near conclusion of the syllogism, as the consequent follows the antecedent, as passed with respect to the first expression, or the conclusion follows from the pronoun, as in the last expression, which is that the corruption of parts of substance is not the corruption of what is not substance. And if added to it: 'and what its corruption is not the corruption of not-substance is substance', it yields: so parts of substance are substance. And perhaps the syllogism returns to producing the desired [conclusion] with the least expression annexed to a premise from its premises, when the premise is taken in the syllogism not in the manner that produces the desired [conclusion] but in a manner that entails it, such as conversion or contrapositive conversion or other than that. So when reduced to what is necessary, its analysis becomes easy. Indeed this premise, which is 'the corruption of what is not substance does not corrupt substance with it', when you say until it becomes 'that which its corruption corrupts substance is substance', it produces directly that parts of substance are substance. And similarly when you say: 'If man exists then animal exists, and if animal exists then substance exists', it yields: 'If man exists then substance exists'. And it has been said about it: 'It is among what is difficult to analyze because it is composed of two conditionals.' Yet there is no exception in it, so it is not a syllogism. And it was said: 'It is difficult as it is from two indeterminate [premises].' And one should in such [cases] preserve things: one that the desired [conclusion] is true; second that it is a necessary consequent of that statement; third that it does not produce that; either because it is not a syllogism or because it is a syllogism and produces something else. These things cannot be preserved in tracking this syllogism because this syllogism is composed of two connected conditionals; and the desired [conclusion] is a connected conditional that does not produce except its identical [form]. So there is no point of criticism in this syllogism except it is said that the two premises are not quantified – not the quantification of the categorical but that of the conditional. So it is fixed if one says instead of 'if' 'whenever'. And if one wants its analysis into two categoricals, that is to say: 'Every man is an animal, every animal is a substance, and every man is a substance'; and it entails: 'If [something] is a man, it is a substance'. This and what is like it...
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a meta-logical discussion on how to analyze syllogisms that appear simple but are actually incomplete or require transformation. The author begins by stating that incomplete syllogisms (القياس الناقص) are difficult to analyze because the mind, hearing the conclusion follow directly from the premises, mistakenly takes the syllogism to be complete and refrains from reducing it to its proper form. The key example is about the parts of substance (أجزاء الجوهر). The argument is: 'Parts of substance: their corruption corrupts the substance. What is not substance: its corruption does not corrupt the substance.' The mind immediately sees that parts of substance are substance, but the syllogism is actually missing a step. The author explains that the desired conclusion is often a necessary consequent of a near conclusion, and that one must supply an implicit premise or convert a premise. He then shows how to reduce it: by adding the premise 'whatever has a corruption that corrupts substance is substance', the conclusion 'parts of substance are substance' follows directly. Next, he considers a conditional syllogism: 'If man exists then animal exists; if animal exists then substance exists; therefore if man exists then substance exists.' This is composed of two conditionals and is not a categorical syllogism because it lacks an exceptive premise. Some say it is difficult because the premises are not quantified in the categorical sense. The author suggests that to avoid criticism, one should replace 'if' with 'whenever' to make it a universal conditional. Alternatively, one can reduce it to two categoricals: 'Every man is an animal; every animal is a substance; therefore every man is a substance', which then entails the conditional. The passage thus provides practical guidance for resolving seemingly intractable syllogisms by identifying implicit conversions or reductions.

القياس الناقص
عكس
عكس نقيض

Reducing Conditional Syllogisms to Categorical Form
Author discusses conditional syllogism and reduction to categoricals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a conditional syllogism composed of two conditionals and convert it into a valid categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
A conditional chain can be reduced to a categorical Barbara by reinterpreting the conditionals as universal statements.

The source presents a conditional argument: 'If man exists then animal exists; if animal exists then substance exists; therefore if man exists then substance exists.' Some scholars find it difficult because it has no exceptive clause and is not a proper qiyas. The author teaches us to replace the conditional 'if' with the universal 'whenever', or to reduce the whole argument to categorical propositions. This lesson shows you how to perform that reduction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premises and conclusion
Premise 1: 'If man exists then animal exists' (إن كان الإنسان موجودا فالحيوان موجود). Premise 2: 'If animal exists then substance exists' (إن كان الحيوان موجودا فالجوهر موجود). Conclusion: 'If man exists then substance exists' (إن كان الإنسان موجودا فالجوهر موجود).
2
Recognize that a universal conditional is equivalent to a categorical
A conditional 'If A then B' when universal (كُلَّما) is logically equivalent to 'Every A is B' (كل A هو B). This allows us to convert the conditionals into categoricals.
The author says 'if one says instead of “if” “whenever”, it is fixed.'
3
Convert both conditionals to categoricals
Replace 'If man exists then animal exists' with 'Every man is an animal'. Replace 'If animal exists then substance exists' with 'Every animal is a substance'.
Now we have two categorical propositions.
4
Combine in a Barbara syllogism
Major: Every animal is a substance. Minor: Every man is an animal. Conclusion: Every man is a substance. This is a valid first-figure syllogism.
Check that the middle term 'animal' appears in both premises.
5
Derive the conditional conclusion from the categorical
From 'Every man is a substance', we can infer the conditional 'If something is a man, then it is a substance'. This matches the original conclusion.
The conditional is entailed by the categorical, so the reduction is successful.

فكان يعتقد أنه لا يمكن تحليله ورفعه إلى ما يلزم منه المطلوب فيبلد فيه . وربَّما كان المطلوب في مثل ذلك لازما للنتيجة القريبة من القياس لزوم التالي للمقدم كما مرَّ بالنسبة إلى العبارة الأولى، أو لزوم النتيجة عن الضمير كما في العبارة الأخيرة وهو أن بطلان أجزاء الجوهر ليس بطلان ما ليس بجوهر . وإذا أضيف إليه وما ليس بطلانه بطلان لا جوهر فهو جوهر فينتج ؛ فأجزاء الجوهر جوهر. وربما كان القياس يعود إلى إنتاج المطلوب بأدنى تعبير يلحق مقدّمة من مقدماته إذا كانت المقدمة مأخوذة في القياس لا على الوجه الذي ينتج المطلوب بل على وجه يلزمه مثل عكس أو عكس نقيض أو غير ذلك. فإذا ردَّ إلى الواجب سهل تحليله فإن هذه المقدمة وهي بطلان ما ليس بجوهر لا يبطل به الجوهر إذا قلت حتى صار ذلك ما يبطل ببطلانه الجوهر فهو جوهر ينتج على الاستقامة أن أجزاء الجوهر جوهر . وكذلك إذا قلت إن كان الإنسان موجودا فالحيوان موجود وإن كان الحيوان موجودا فالجوهر موجود فينتج إن كان الإنسان موجودا فالجوهر موجود. وقد قيل فيه: «إنه من جملة ما يعسر تحليله لما أنه يتألف من شرطيتين». ومع ذلك فليس فيه استثناء فليس بقياس، وقيل: «إنه يعسر كما أنه من المهملتين. وينبغي أن يكون ما يقال في مثل هذا الحفظ أشياء أحدها أن يكون المطلوب حقًا، والثاني أن يكون لازما غير ذلك القول، والثالث أنه ليس ينتج ذلك؛ إما لأنه غير قياس وإما لأنه قياس، وينتج غير ذلك: فهذه الأشياء لا يمكن أن تحفظ في تعقب هذا القياس لأن هذا القياس مؤلف من شرطيتين متصلتين؛ والمطلوب شرطي متصل لا ينتج إلا عينه فلا يوجد في هذا القياس مغمز إلا أن يقال إن المقدمتين ليستا محصورتين لا الحصر الذي للحملي بل الذي للشرطي. فيصلح إذا قيل بدل إن كان كلما، وإذا أريد تحليله إلى الحمليتين فذلك أن يقال إن كل إنسان حيوان، وكل حيوان جوهر وكل إنسان جوهر ؛ ويلزمه إن كان إنسانًا فهو جوهر، فهذا وما هو بمثله من
Section: On the mention of syllogistic compositions that are difficult to analyze and mention of what facilitates that. As for the mention of syllogistic compositions that are difficult to analyze and mention of what facilitates that, that is among the necessities: often the analysis of an incomplete syllogism is difficult when the apparent heard [statement] from it entails the conclusion in the mind, so he thinks it is complete and refrains from reducing it to its completion. Then we do not find the existing number participating in the conclusion in a participation that should be between it and the premises. An example of that: parts of substance: their corruption corrupts the substance, and what is not a substance, its corruption does not corrupt the substance. Or we say: corruption of parts of substance corrupts substance with it, and corruption of what is not substance does not corrupt substance with it, so it follows that parts of substance are substance. Then the mind, when it believes it is complete, this follows, so he thinks it cannot be analyzed and raised to what entails the desired [conclusion], so he is confused in it. And perhaps the desired [conclusion] in such [cases] is a necessary consequent of the near conclusion of the syllogism, as the consequent follows the antecedent, as passed with respect to the first expression, or the conclusion follows from the pronoun, as in the last expression, which is that the corruption of parts of substance is not the corruption of what is not substance. And if added to it: 'and what its corruption is not the corruption of not-substance is substance', it yields: so parts of substance are substance. And perhaps the syllogism returns to producing the desired [conclusion] with the least expression annexed to a premise from its premises, when the premise is taken in the syllogism not in the manner that produces the desired [conclusion] but in a manner that entails it, such as conversion or contrapositive conversion or other than that. So when reduced to what is necessary, its analysis becomes easy. Indeed this premise, which is 'the corruption of what is not substance does not corrupt substance with it', when you say until it becomes 'that which its corruption corrupts substance is substance', it produces directly that parts of substance are substance. And similarly when you say: 'If man exists then animal exists, and if animal exists then substance exists', it yields: 'If man exists then substance exists'. And it has been said about it: 'It is among what is difficult to analyze because it is composed of two conditionals.' Yet there is no exception in it, so it is not a syllogism. And it was said: 'It is difficult as it is from two indeterminate [premises].' And one should in such [cases] preserve things: one that the desired [conclusion] is true; second that it is a necessary consequent of that statement; third that it does not produce that; either because it is not a syllogism or because it is a syllogism and produces something else. These things cannot be preserved in tracking this syllogism because this syllogism is composed of two connected conditionals; and the desired [conclusion] is a connected conditional that does not produce except its identical [form]. So there is no point of criticism in this syllogism except it is said that the two premises are not quantified – not the quantification of the categorical but that of the conditional. So it is fixed if one says instead of 'if' 'whenever'. And if one wants its analysis into two categoricals, that is to say: 'Every man is an animal, every animal is a substance, and every man is a substance'; and it entails: 'If [something] is a man, it is a substance'. This and what is like it...
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a meta-logical discussion on how to analyze syllogisms that appear simple but are actually incomplete or require transformation. The author begins by stating that incomplete syllogisms (القياس الناقص) are difficult to analyze because the mind, hearing the conclusion follow directly from the premises, mistakenly takes the syllogism to be complete and refrains from reducing it to its proper form. The key example is about the parts of substance (أجزاء الجوهر). The argument is: 'Parts of substance: their corruption corrupts the substance. What is not substance: its corruption does not corrupt the substance.' The mind immediately sees that parts of substance are substance, but the syllogism is actually missing a step. The author explains that the desired conclusion is often a necessary consequent of a near conclusion, and that one must supply an implicit premise or convert a premise. He then shows how to reduce it: by adding the premise 'whatever has a corruption that corrupts substance is substance', the conclusion 'parts of substance are substance' follows directly. Next, he considers a conditional syllogism: 'If man exists then animal exists; if animal exists then substance exists; therefore if man exists then substance exists.' This is composed of two conditionals and is not a categorical syllogism because it lacks an exceptive premise. Some say it is difficult because the premises are not quantified in the categorical sense. The author suggests that to avoid criticism, one should replace 'if' with 'whenever' to make it a universal conditional. Alternatively, one can reduce it to two categoricals: 'Every man is an animal; every animal is a substance; therefore every man is a substance', which then entails the conditional. The passage thus provides practical guidance for resolving seemingly intractable syllogisms by identifying implicit conversions or reductions.

Key Terms
شرطيتان متصلتان
حمليتان
عكس نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what resolves the difficulty of analyzing the conditional syllogism?
A) Adding an exceptive premise
B) Replacing 'if' with 'whenever' or reducing to categoricals
C) Converting one premise by contraposition
D) Assuming the premises are false

Key Takeaway
A conditional syllogism without an exceptive clause can be validated by converting the conditionals into universal categoricals and constructing a Barbara syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reduce the following conditional argument to categoricals: 'If the prayer is invalid (باطلة), then the ablution is invalid; if the ablution is invalid, then the purification is invalid; therefore if the prayer is invalid, the purification is invalid.'
?
Explain why the conditional 'If it rains then the ground is wet; if the ground is wet then it is slippery; therefore if it rains then it is slippery' is valid but not considered a proper qiyas in the Islamic logical tradition. (Hint: no exception, and the premises are not quantified.)

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Syllogisms Requiring Premise Conversion
Author explains how a premise may be taken in a way that entails the required conclusion through conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a syllogism requires premise conversion and perform the necessary transformation to produce the conclusion.

Core Idea
Syllogisms often need premise conversion before yielding their intended conclusion.

The author demonstrates that a syllogism's premises may not directly entail the desired conclusion; instead, one premise might need to be converted (e.g., by simple conversion or contrapositive). This lesson examines how to spot such situations and apply the correct transformation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
Determine what the syllogism is supposed to prove. In the example, it is 'the parts of substance are substance'.
2
Check if premises directly produce it
Examine the given premise: 'The annihilation of the parts of substance is not the annihilation of what is not substance.' Does it directly combine with another premise to yield the conclusion?
3
Apply conversion to the premise
Convert the premise as needed. The author converts it to a contrapositive-like form: 'What its annihilation is not the annihilation of substance, then it is substance.'
Conversion types include simple conversion (عكس) and contrapositive (عكس نقيض).
4
Reconstruct the syllogism
With the converted premise, the syllogism now produces the conclusion directly: 'the parts of substance are substance.' Verify the validity.
5
Generalize the technique
Recognize that any syllogism where a premise is not in the required form can be analyzed by converting it to its necessary equivalent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنطقة الكبيرة أو لزوم النتيجة عن الضمير كما في العبارة الأخيرة وهو أن بطلان أجزاء الجوهر ليس بطلان ما ليس بجوهر. وإذا أضيف إليه وما ليس بطلانه بطلان لا جوهر فهو جوهر فينتج؛ فأجزاء الجوهر جوهر. وربما كان القياس يعود إلى إنتاج المطلوب بأدنى تعبير يلحق مقدمة من مقدماته إذا كانت المقدمة مأخوذة في القياس لا على الوجه الذي ينتج المطلوب بل على وجه يلزمه مثل عكس أو عكس نقيض أو غير ذلك. فإذا رد إلى الواجب سهل تحليله فإن هذه المقدمة وهي بطلان ما ليس بجوهر لا يبطل به الجوهر إذا قلت حتى صار ذلك ما يبطل ببطلانه الجوهر فهو جوهر ينتج على الاستقامة أن أجزاء الجوهر جوهر. وكذلك إذا قلت إن كان الإنسان موجودا فالحيوان موجود وإن كان الحيوان موجودا فالجوهر موجود فينتج إن كان الإنسان موجودا فالجوهر موجود. وقد قيل فيه: «إنه من جملة ما يعسر تحليله لما أنه يتألف من شرطيتين». ومع ذلك فليس فيه استثناء فليس بقياس، وقيل: «إنه يعسر كما أنه من المهملتين. وينبغي أن يكون ما يقال في مثل هذا الحفظ أشياء أحدها أن يكون المطلوب حقا، والثاني أن يكون لازما غير ذلك القول، والثالث أنه ليس ينتج ذلك؛ إما لأنه غير قياس وإما لأنه قياس، وينتج غير ذلك: فهذه الأشياء لا يمكن أن تحفظ في تعقب هذا القياس لأن هذا القياس مؤلف من شرطيتين متصلتين؛ والمطلوب شرطي متصل لا ينتج إلا عينه فلا يوجد في هذا القياس مغمز إلا أن يقال إن المقدمتين ليستا محصورتين لا الحصر الذي للحملي بل الذي للشرطي. فيصلح إذا قيل بدل إن كان كلما، وإذا أريد تحليله إلى الحمليتين فذلك أن يقال إن كل إنسان حيوان، وكل حيوان جوهر وكل إنسان جوهر؛ ويلزمه إن كان إنسانا فهو جوهر، فهذا وما هو بمثله من
The major region or the necessity of the conclusion from the conscience, as in the last expression: that the annihilation of the parts of substance is not the annihilation of what is not substance. And if added to it, 'And what its annihilation is not the annihilation of substance, then it is substance,' it produces: so the parts of substance are substance. And perhaps the syllogism returns to producing the required in the least expression, attaching a premise from its premises if the premise is taken in the syllogism not in the way that produces the required but in a way that entails it, like conversion or contrapositive conversion or otherwise. So when reduced to the necessary, its analysis becomes easy, for this premise—'The annihilation of what is not substance does not annihilate substance'—if you say until that becomes what is annihilated by its annihilation is substance, it produces directly that the parts of substance are substance. Likewise, if you say: 'If man exists, then animal exists; and if animal exists, then substance exists,' it produces: 'If man exists, then substance exists.' And it was said about it: 'It is among what is difficult to analyze because it is composed of two conditionals.' Nevertheless, there is no exception in it, so it is not a syllogism. And it was said: 'It is difficult as it is among the unquantifieds. And what should be said in such preservation are things, one of which is that the required is true, the second is that it is a necessary consequence of that statement, and the third is that it does not produce that; either because it is not a syllogism, or because it is a syllogism and produces something else. So these things cannot be preserved in tracking this syllogism because this syllogism is composed of two connected conditionals, and the required is a connected conditional that produces nothing but itself, so there is no point of attack in this syllogism except to say that the two premises are not quantified—not the quantification of the categorical but that of the conditional. So it becomes valid if one says instead of 'if' 'whenever', and if one wants to analyze it into two categoricals, that one says: 'Every man is an animal, and every animal is a substance, and every man is a substance'; and it entails that 'if he is a man, he is a substance.' This and what is like it is from the category of what appears to be a syllogism and is not in reality a syllogism.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on how syllogisms can be analyzed when their premises are not in the standard form. The author begins by referring to a previous expression about the 'major region' (al-mantiqa al-kabira) and the necessity of the conclusion from the conscience. He then gives an example: if you say 'The annihilation of the parts of substance is not the annihilation of what is not substance' and add 'What its annihilation is not the annihilation of substance, then it is substance', you get the conclusion 'The parts of substance are substance'. This shows how a premise might be taken in a way that entails the conclusion through conversion or contrapositive. The author then explains that by reducing such premises to their necessary form, analysis becomes easy. He gives another example with conditionals: 'If man exists, then animal exists; if animal exists, then substance exists' entails 'If man exists, then substance exists'. He notes that some say this conditional syllogism is difficult to analyze because it consists of two conditionals. He points out that there is no exception, so it is not a proper syllogism. The author then states three things to preserve when evaluating such arguments: (1) the required conclusion must be true, (2) it should necessarily follow from the statement, and (3) either the argument does not produce the conclusion because it is not a syllogism, or it is a syllogism that produces something else. These checks are difficult to apply here because the syllogism is composed of two connected conditionals and the desired conclusion is itself a connected conditional that only produces itself. The only point of attack is that the premises are not universally quantified in the way of categoricals but in the way of conditionals. To fix this, one can replace 'if' with 'whenever' (a universal quantifier for conditionals) or convert the conditionals into categoricals. For example, 'Every man is an animal, every animal is a substance' yields 'Every man is a substance', which entails the original conditional conclusion. The author concludes that such constructions appear to be syllogisms but are not real syllogisms.

لزوم النتيجة
عكس
عكس نقيض

C) Obversion

Key Takeaway
Syllogisms often require immediate inference (conversion) on a premise to expose the logical connection to the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: Take the premise 'Everything that is created is contingent.' Convert it to its contrapositive and form a categorical syllogism with 'The world is created' to prove 'The world is contingent.'
?

The Syllogism

Analyzing Conditional Syllogisms
Author discusses the difficulty of analyzing conditional syllogisms and provides methods to convert them into valid categorical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to transform a conditional syllogism into a valid categorical syllogism and identify when a conditional chain is not a true syllogism.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms without proper quantification are not genuine syllogisms.

The author points out that a chain of conditionals like 'If A then B, if B then C' does not automatically produce 'If A then C' as a valid syllogism. Only by replacing 'if' with 'whenever' (universal quantifier) or converting to categoricals do we get a valid argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premises
In the example, we have two connected conditionals: 'If man exists, then animal exists' and 'If animal exists, then substance exists.'
2
Notice the lack of quantification
The conditionals use 'if' without universal quantification. This makes the argument invalid as a syllogism because it does not guarantee the connection for all instances.
3
Apply the first fix: universal quantifier
Replace 'if' with 'whenever' (كلما) to obtain universally quantified conditionals: 'Whenever man exists, animal exists; whenever animal exists, substance exists.' Now the conclusion 'Whenever man exists, substance exists' follows validly.
4
Apply the second fix: convert to categoricals
Convert the conditionals into categorical form: 'Every man is an animal; every animal is a substance.' Then the categorical syllogism yields 'Every man is a substance,' which entails the original conditional conclusion.
5
Assess the original argument
Without these fixes, the original conditional chain is not a valid syllogism. The author calls it 'difficult to analyze' and notes it is not a true syllogism because there is no exception clause and it is unquantified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

A) Replace 'if' with 'whenever' to quantify the conditionals universally.
B) Convert the conditionals into categoricals: 'Every man is an animal; every animal is a substance; therefore every man is a substance'.
C) Add an exception clause to the conditionals.
D) Both A and B are suggested.

Key Takeaway
Conditional syllogisms without universal quantification are not genuine syllogisms; they can be fixed by quantifying the conditionals or converting to categoricals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: Convert the conditional syllogism 'If wine is intoxicating, then it is forbidden; if it is forbidden, then its sale is invalid; therefore if wine is intoxicating, its sale is invalid' into a valid categorical syllogism.
?
Task: In the author's example, explain why the argument is not a valid syllogism without the quantification fix, and state what the two fixes are.

Identifying Apparent Syllogisms: The Condition of Universal Quantification
Author discusses apparent syllogisms and the condition of universal quantification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an apparent syllogism by detecting whether the major premise is universally quantified.

Core Idea
An apparent syllogism looks like a proof but fails because a condition is missing.

In this passage, the author distinguishes a true syllogism from an apparent one. The key condition is that the major premise must be universal. We will examine the example of 'Zayd is imagining Zayd; the imagined can be eternal' to see why it fails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the attempted syllogism
Find the premises and conclusion in the example: minor premise 'Zayd is imagining Zayd', major premise 'the imagined thing can possibly be eternal', conclusion 'Zayd can possibly be eternal'.
2
Check the quantification of the major premise
The major premise 'the imagined thing can possibly be eternal' is not quantified (muhmala). It lacks 'every' or 'all'. A true syllogism requires a universal major premise.
The Arabic term for unquantified is مهملة (muhmala).
3
Consider making it universal
If we change it to 'Every imagined thing can possibly be eternal', the premise becomes false, so the syllogism still fails. Thus, the condition includes both quantification and truth.
4
Apply to other examples
Use this method to test any argument: is the major premise universal? If not, it is an apparent syllogism.
In Islamic sciences, always check the quantification of the major premise in a syllogism.

جملة ما يوهم أنه قياس؛ وليس بالحقيقة قياسا وقد كان القياس ما يلزم عن لذاته المطلوب. وقد يعرض أن يكون الموهم شيء فيه بنفسه لا بالقياس إلى ما يظن أنه يلزم عنه. وذلك لمشابهة حدود لحدود القياس مع إحلال وقع فيه الشرط أو لشروط تلحق الحدود بين الأسوار والروابط وغير ذلك. ولا يلزم منه شيء فيظن قياسا مثاله إن زيدا متوهم زيدا، والمتوهم قد يمكن أن يكون أزليا وقيل فيه إنه لا ينتج لفوت الشرط وهو كون الكبرى كلية إذا الكبرى مهملة فيه. فلو قلت وكل متوهم يمكن أن يكون أزليا كان القول مشتملة على الشرط لكنه يكون كاذبا فلا ينتج أيضا. ولقائل أن يقول إن الكبرى ليست مهملة بل شخصية والقياسات المؤلفة من الشخصيات قد تكون منتجة فإنه إذا قيل إن زيدا هذا القاعد، وهذا القاعد أبيض؛ لزم دائما أن يكون زيد أبيض. وأما أن الجزئي لا ينتج حيث تكون جزئية محصورة أو في قوتها. فلو قلت إن زيدا متوهم زيدا فإن كان متوهم زيدا شخصيا ثم كرر وصدق أن المتوهم زيدا يمكن أن يكون أزليا فيكون القياس منتجا، وإن كان قولنا هذا المتوهم زيدا يمكن أن يكون أزليا كاذبا فإنه لا ينتج لكونه كاذبا لا لغيره. ثم لقائل أن يقول أيضا إن قولنا المتوهم زيدا يمكن أن يفهم منه الشيء الموجود خارجا تضاف إليه صورة في النفس وهمية، ويمكن أن يفهم منه نفس تلك الصورة التي في الوهم فإنه هو المتوهم عن زيد؛ فالمعنى الأول إذا أضيف إليه أنه يمكن أن يكون أزليا؛ يمكن أن يفهم منه أن يكون دائم الوجود في نفسه، ويمكن أن يفهم أنه يكون دائم الوجود في الوهم، ويمكن أن يفهم منه أنه متوهم محكوما عليه أن دائم الوجود لا على معنى أنه كذلك في الوجود وما يطابق المعنى الأول من
A summary of what appears to be a syllogism, but is not in reality a syllogism, whereas a syllogism is that which necessitates the required by itself. And it may happen that the apparent thing has something in itself, not in relation to what it is thought to entail. That is due to similarity of terms to terms of the syllogism, with a substitution that occurred in the condition, or due to conditions that attach to the terms between the quantifiers and connectives and other things. And nothing entails from it, so it is thought to be a syllogism. Example: 'Zayd is imagining Zayd', and the imagined thing can possibly be eternal. And it was said about it that it does not produce due to the loss of the condition, which is that the major premise is universal, when the major premise is unquantified in it. So if you said, 'Every imagining thing can possibly be eternal,' the statement would include the condition, but it would be false, so it does not produce either. And a speaker could say that the major premise is not unquantified but particular, and syllogisms composed of particulars can be productive, for if it is said 'Zayd is this sitting one, and this sitting one is white,' it always entails that Zayd is white. But as for the particular not producing when it is a quantified particular or in its force. So if you said, 'Zayd is imagining Zayd,' then if 'Zayd is imagining Zayd' is particular, and then you repeated and verified that 'the imagining of Zayd can possibly be eternal,' then the syllogism would be productive. And if our statement 'This imagining of Zayd can possibly be eternal' is false, then it does not produce because of its falsehood, not for other reasons. Then a speaker could also say that our statement 'The imagining of Zayd' can be understood as the external thing to which an imaginary form in the soul is added, and it can be understood as the form itself that is in the imagination, which is the imagined thing about Zayd. So the first meaning, if added to it that it can be eternal, can be understood as it being perpetual in existence in itself, and it can be understood as it being perpetual in existence in the imagination, and it can be understood as it being imagined, judged to be perpetual in existence, not in the sense that it is so in existence, and what matches the first meaning from
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is about apparent syllogisms (mā yūhim annahu qiyās) that are not real syllogisms. The author begins by contrasting them with true syllogisms, which necessarily entail the required conclusion by themselves. Apparent syllogisms may have something in themselves that causes the illusion, such as similarity of terms to those of a real syllogism but with a substitution in the condition or additional conditions attached to quantifiers and connectives. An example is given: 'Zayd is imagining Zayd' and 'The imagined thing can possibly be eternal.' This fails to produce a conclusion because the major premise ('the imagined thing can possibly be eternal') is not universal—it is unquantified (muhmala). Even if we make it universal ('Every imagining thing can possibly be eternal'), it becomes false, so still no productive syllogism. A counterargument is raised: the major premise might be particular (shakhṣiyya), not unquantified. Particular syllogisms can be productive, as in 'Zayd is this sitting one, this sitting one is white, so Zayd is white.' However, the author clarifies that a particular premise does not produce a conclusion when it is a quantified particular (juzʾiyya maḥṣūra) or in its force. The example is then refined: if 'Zayd is imagining Zayd' is a particular statement, and we add the true premise 'the imagining of Zayd can possibly be eternal,' then the syllogism would be productive. But if the latter premise is false, it fails due to falsehood. The author then notes an ambiguity: 'the imagining of Zayd' can mean either the external Zayd with an imaginary form attached, or the imaginary form itself. Depending on the meaning, the possibility of being eternal can be interpreted as eternal existence in reality, or eternal existence in the imagination, or as being imagined and judged eternal in a certain sense. The passage cuts off while discussing this ambiguity.

مهملة
جزئية محصورة

Why does the syllogism 'Zayd is imagining Zayd; the imagined thing can possibly be eternal; therefore Zayd can possibly be eternal' fail according to the author?
A) The major premise is false regardless of quantification.
B) The major premise is unquantified, so no necessary conclusion follows.
C) The minor premise is particular and cannot be used in a syllogism.
D) The term 'Zayd' is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
A valid categorical syllogism requires the major premise to be universally quantified; otherwise, it is merely an apparent syllogism.

Particular Premises and Ambiguity in Apparent Syllogisms
Author discusses objections about particular premises and ambiguity in the example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a valid particular syllogism and an apparent syllogism with a quantified particular premise.

Core Idea
Not all particular premises are equal; a quantified particular can yield a valid syllogism, while unquantified ones cannot.

The author anticipates an objection: that the major premise might be particular (shakhṣiyya) and still productive. He clarifies that only quantified particulars (juzʾiyya maḥṣūra) work. Additionally, he reveals an ambiguity in the term 'imagining of Zayd' that can lead to multiple interpretations, affecting the syllogism's validity.

Guided Path
1
Examine the objection to the major premise
A potential objector says the major premise 'the imagined thing can possibly be eternal' could be particular (شخصية), not unquantified. For example, 'Zayd is this sitting one; this sitting one is white' produces a conclusion. Understand this objection.
The author concedes that particular syllogisms can be productive, but only under certain conditions.
2
Differentiate between unquantified and quantified particular
The author stresses that a particular premise must be quantified (محصورة) or in its force. An unquantified particular like 'some imagined thing can be eternal' is still insufficient. A quantified particular like 'this specific imagined thing' (if it refers to a single object) can work.
Arabic: جزئية محصورة means a particular proposition that specifies a definite individual or a quantified 'some'.
3
Analyze the ambiguity of 'imagining of Zayd'
The phrase can refer either to the external Zayd with an imagined attribute, or to the mental image itself. Depending on the interpretation, the possibility of being eternal can mean eternal existence in reality, eternal existence in the imagination, or being judged as eternal in the mind. This ambiguity affects whether the premise is true.
The passage cuts off, but the author hints that clarifying the meaning can turn the apparent syllogism into a valid one.
