Identifying Pseudo-Syllogisms: The Case of Zayd the Imagined
Author analyzes a false syllogism about Zayd being imagined and refutes objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an argument is a pseudo-syllogism by checking the conditions of universality, truth, and term consistency.

Core Idea
A pseudo-syllogism looks like a genuine syllogism but fails due to missing conditions or ambiguous terms.

In this passage, the author analyzes an argument that appears to be a syllogism but is not. The example involves Zayd being imagined and whether he could be eternal. By dissecting this case, we learn the essential conditions for a valid syllogism: the major premise must be universal, true, and terms must be used unambiguously. Let's walk through the analysis step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pseudo-syllogism
Locate the argument: 'Zayd is imagined as Zayd; what is imagined may be eternal.' This is presented as a candidate syllogism, but the author says it is not valid.
The conclusion is implied: therefore Zayd may be eternal.
2
Check the condition of universality
The major premise 'what is imagined may be eternal' is indefinite (مهملة), not universal. For a categorical syllogism to produce a conclusion, the major premise must be universal. The author states that this loss of condition prevents the syllogism from being productive.
If we make it universal ('every imagined thing may be eternal'), it becomes false.
3
Examine the objection about particular premises
An objector argues that the major premise could be particular (شخصية) and still productive. The author concedes that particular premises can work but only if they are truly particular. In this case, 'Zayd is imagined as Zayd' is particular, but the major 'this imagined Zayd may be eternal' must be true. If false, failure is due to falsehood, not form.
The author gives an example: 'Zayd is the one sitting, and this one sitting is white; therefore Zayd is white' – a valid syllogism with particular premises.
4
Analyze term ambiguity
The phrase 'the imagined Zayd' is ambiguous. It could refer to (1) the external object, (2) the mental image, or (3) the judgment in the imagination. Depending on the meaning, the major premise changes truth value. This ambiguity leads to a fallacy of equivocation.
The author concludes that the middle term is actually universal if interpreted correctly, but the ambiguities cause the syllogism to fail.

قولنا المتوهم زيدا يمكن أن يفهم منه الشيء الموجود خارجًا تضاف إليه صورة في النفس وهمية، ويمكن أن يفهم منه نفس تلك الصورة التي في الوهم فإنه هو المتوهم عن زيد؛ فالمعنى الأول إذا أضيف إليه أنه يمكن (أن) يكون أزليّا؛ يمكن أن يفهم منه أن يكون دائم الوجود في نفسه، ويمكن أن يفهم أنه يكون دائم الوجود في الوهم، ويمكن أن يفهم منه أنه متوهم محكوما عليه أن دائم الوجود لا على معنى أنه كذلك في الوجود وما يطابق المعنى الأول من الألفاظ فذلك أن الشيء الحاصل صورته في الوهم موجود في الأعيان دائما. والذي يطابق المعنى الذي هو أن الشيء الحاصل في الوهم صورته تبقى صورته في الوهم دائما بقي هو أو لم يبق . والذي يطابق المعنى الثالث هو أن الشيء الفلاني قد أحضر في الوهم صورته وحكم على أنها دائمة الوجود في الأعيان حكما في الوهم فإن عني به المعنى الأول أو الثاني فالقضية كاذبة، وإن عني به الثالث فهناك تقصير في العبارة؛ إذ معنى القول إن المتوهم زيد أزلي في حكم الوهم فيجب أن يوجد الأدنى كذلك في النتيجة فتكون النتيجة صادقة. والسبب في كذب النتيجة هو أن الحدود في القياس على نحو وفي النتيجة على نحو لا أن ترتيبها ترتيب رديء؛ فيجب أن نحكم الآن في ذلك فنقول أما قولهم متوهم زيدا فهو معنى يمكن أن يفهم على وجه كليًّا، وذلك بأن يفهم منه أن هذا الشيء حصلت صورته في الوهم منسوبة إليه قد يمكن في التوهم العام أن يتوهم غير زيد زيدًا كاذبا كان أو صادقاً. وفرق بين أن يكون الشيء قولا وبين أن يكون صادقًا، وبين أن يكون متوهما وبين أن يكون صادقًا، فإذن الأوسط على هذا الاعتبار كلي.
A summary of what gives the impression of being a syllogism, but is not in reality a syllogism. A syllogism is that which entails by itself the desired [conclusion]. Sometimes the thing that gives the impression may be in itself [something else], not by reference to what it is thought to entail. That is due to the similarity of terms to terms of a syllogism with a substitution that occurs in the condition or conditions that attach to the terms among the quantifiers and connectives and other things. Nothing follows from it, so it is thought to be a syllogism. An example: 'Zayd is imagined as Zayd, and what is imagined may be eternal.' It was said about it that it does not produce [a conclusion] due to the loss of the condition, which is that the major premise be universal, since the major premise is indefinite in it. If you said: 'And every imagined thing may be eternal,' then the statement contains the condition, but it would be false, so it also does not produce. And one may say that the major premise is not indefinite but particular, and syllogisms composed of particulars may be productive; for if it is said: 'Zayd is the one sitting, and this one sitting is white,' it necessarily follows always that Zayd is white. As for the particular not producing when it is a quantified particular or in its force, if you said: 'Zayd is imagined as Zayd,' then if the imagined Zayd is particular, and then it is repeated and it is verified that the imagined Zayd may be eternal, then the syllogism is productive. And if our statement 'this imagined Zayd may be eternal' is false, then it does not produce because of its falsehood, not for something else. Then one may also say that our statement 'the imagined Zayd' may be understood to mean the external thing to which an imaginary form in the soul is attached, and it may be understood to mean that same form in the imagination, for it is what is imagined about Zayd. So the first meaning, if it is added that it may be eternal, it may be understood to mean that it is always existing in itself, and it may be understood to mean that it is always existing in the imagination, and it may be understood to mean that it is something imagined that is judged to be always existing, not in the sense that it is so in existence. And what corresponds to the first meaning among the expressions is that the thing whose form is obtained in the imagination exists in external things always. And what corresponds to the second meaning is that the thing whose form is obtained in the imagination, its form remains in the imagination always, whether it remains or not. And what corresponds to the third meaning is that the thing in question has its form brought into the imagination and it is judged that it is always existing in external things as a judgment in the imagination. If the first or second meaning is intended, the proposition is false. If the third is intended, there is a shortcoming in the expression; for the meaning of the statement 'the imagined Zayd is eternal' is in the judgment of the imagination, so the minor must be found likewise in the conclusion, so the conclusion would be true. The reason for the falsehood of the conclusion is that the terms in the syllogism are in one way and in the conclusion in another way, not that its arrangement is a bad arrangement. So we must now judge that and say: As for their statement 'imagined Zayd,' it is a meaning that can be understood in a universal manner, and that is by understanding from it that this thing has its form obtained in the imagination attributed to it; indeed, in general imagination it is possible that someone other than Zayd may imagine Zayd, whether falsely or truly. And there is a difference between a thing being a statement and being true, and between being imagined and being true. Therefore, the middle term, on this consideration, is universal.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage examines a pseudo-syllogism—an argument that appears to be a valid syllogism but is not. The author begins by defining a true syllogism as one that entails the desired conclusion by its very nature. A pseudo-syllogism may arise due to formal similarity of terms, but with a missing condition (e.g., regarding quantifiers or connectives). The example given is: 'Zayd is imagined as Zayd; and what is imagined may be eternal.' At first glance, this seems to be a syllogism, but it fails because the major premise is indefinite (مهملة), not universal. If we make it universal—'Every imagined thing may be eternal'—it becomes false, so still no conclusion. The author then anticipates an objection: the major might be particular (شخصية), and syllogisms with particular premises can be productive, as in 'Zayd is the one sitting; this one sitting is white; therefore Zayd is white.' However, the objector must note that a particular premise only works if it is truly particular (جزئية محصورة) or in its force. If 'Zayd is imagined as Zayd' is particular and true, and the major 'this imagined Zayd may be eternal' is true, then the syllogism would be productive. But if the major is false, the failure is due to falsehood, not form. The author then deepens the analysis by noting that 'the imagined Zayd' is ambiguous. It could mean: (1) the external thing that has an imaginary form in the mind; (2) the imaginary form itself in the mind; or (3) the thing that is imagined and judged to be eternal within the imagination. Depending on the meaning, the major premise can be true or false. If meaning (1) or (2) is intended, the statement 'the imagined Zayd is eternal' is false (since external things or mental images are not eternal). If meaning (3) is intended, the expression is deficient: the judgment is only within the imagination, so the conclusion would be true only if interpreted accordingly. The key insight is that the falsehood of the conclusion arises because the terms in the premises and conclusion are used in different senses, not because the syllogistic form is inherently flawed. Finally, the author concludes that 'imagined Zayd' can be understood as a universal term, since in general imagination one can imagine Zayd falsely or truly. Thus the middle term is universal on this interpretation, and the student must carefully parse the different possible meanings.

Key Terms
الموهم (al-muwahim): That which gives the impression of being a syllogism but is not actually one.
القياس (al-qiyas): A syllogism that entails the desired conclusion by itself.
الشرط (al-shart): The condition that must be present for the syllogism to be productive, e.g., universality of the major premise.
كبرى (kubra): The major premise of the syllogism.
شخصية (shakhsiyya): A particular proposition referring to a specific individual, as opposed to universal.
متوهم (mutawahham): Something that is imagined, either as an external object or as a mental image.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'Zayd is imagined as Zayd; what is imagined may be eternal,' why does the argument fail to produce a conclusion?
A) Because the major premise is false.
B) Because the major premise is indefinite, not universal.
C) Because the minor premise is false.
D) Because the middle term is not distributed.

Key Takeaway
A pseudo-syllogism fails not because of its form but because of missing conditions like universality, truth, or term univocity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a pseudo-syllogism on an Islamic topic (e.g., 'The Quran is recited; what is recited is created; therefore the Quran is created') and explain which condition(s) are missing (universality, truth, or term clarity).
?

Modal Conditions and Syllogistic Fallacies
Author discusses errors from relational terms and modal qualifiers in syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a syllogistic conclusion depends on the modal qualifiers of its premises and detect the fallacy of ignoring modality.

Core Idea
Modality is not decoration—it can make a valid argument fallacious if ignored.

In this passage, the author warns that a syllogism's conclusion is not always necessary just because the argument seems valid. For example, from 'The conclusion is not in anything of illness' and 'illness is in every human', one might think 'health is in no human'. But the author explains that this conclusion is necessary only if the minor premise is necessary. This lesson will train you to check modality and avoid such fallacies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism's structure
Extract the two premises and the purported conclusion from the example: Premise 1: 'Health is not in anything of illness.' Premise 2: 'Illness is in every human.' Conclusion: 'Health is not in any human.' Note that the conclusion seems to follow from these premises.
This is a categorical syllogism with health, illness, and human as terms.
2
Determine the modal status of the minor premise
The author focuses on the minor premise 'illness is in every human'. Ask: Is this statement necessary, possible, or absolute (without modality)? In the example, if it is necessary (i.e., illness is necessarily in every human), then the conclusion is necessary. If it is only possible or absolute, the conclusion follows proportionally.
The minor premise is 'illness is in every human'.
3
Check if the conclusion matches the premise's modality
The fallacy occurs when one assumes the conclusion is necessary without verifying the modality of the minor premise. If the minor premise is not necessary, the conclusion cannot be asserted as necessary; it must be qualified accordingly.
4
Identify the type of error
This is a modal fallacy—specifically, the fallacy of ignoring modality (or modal equivocation). The author also warns against substituting a state for its subject (e.g., 'health' for 'healthy'), which is an error of relation or category mistake.
The passage also mentions errors from quantification (hasr) and omission (ihmal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربَّما كان التخليط في روابط الحدود. وذلك لأنه ربَّما يعبر عن القياس بأن ابتدئ من المحمولات حينئذ إلى زيادة لفظ ليس داخلا في الحدود بل هو رابطة أو نسبة رابطة ؛ ثمَّ يقع فيه اشتراك كقول من يقول إن النتيجة لا في شيء من المرض، والمرض في كل إنسان قبل، وقد يظن أنه يعرض من هذا أن الصحة غير ممكنة أن تكون في واحد من الناس. ثم لقائل أن يقول: إنه إحدى النتيجة ضرورية ولا يلزم أن يكون كذلك وإن كان قياسًا. والذي يظن أن يلزم عنه هو أن الصحة ليست ولا في واحد من الناس فإنه يكون حقا إذا كانت الصغرى وهي قولنا إن المرض في كل إنسان ضرورية. فأما إذا كانت ممكنة خاصة فإنه يجب أن يكون ممكنا، وكذلك إذا كانت الصغرى مطلقة وصح إطلاقها فإنه يجب أن يكون مطلقا . ثم الحالة إذا أخذت مكان ما يقبلها كما إذا أخذت الصحة مكان الصحيح والمرض مكان المريض في القوة وإن لم يكن بالفعل، فهذه أنحاء من الغلط يعرض بسبب الأمور الداخلة على الحدود مثل الحصر والإهمال والربط.
And perhaps confusion occurs in the relations of the terms. That is because sometimes the syllogism is expressed by beginning from the predicates, then adding a word that is not part of the terms but is a relation or relative connection; then ambiguity occurs in it, like someone who says: 'The conclusion is not in anything of illness, and illness is in every human as a prior,' and one might think that from this it follows that health is impossible to be in any of the people. Then a speaker can say: It is one necessarily of the conclusion, and it is not necessary that it be so even if it is a syllogism. And what one thinks follows from it is that health is not in any of the people, and it would be true if the minor premise—namely our saying 'illness is in every human'—is necessary. But if it is possible in a particular way, then it must be possible; and likewise if the minor premise is absolute and its absoluteness holds, then it must be absolute. Then, if the state is taken in place of what receives it, such as when 'health' is taken in place of 'healthy' and 'illness' in place of 'ill' potentially if not actually, these are types of error that occur due to matters entering upon the terms, such as quantification, omission, and relation.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage identifies fallacies in syllogisms arising from the relations between terms (rawabit al-hudud). The author gives an example: someone constructs a syllogism with 'the conclusion is not in anything of illness' and 'illness is in every human prior'—this is a modal syllogism. It might seem to imply that health cannot be in any human, which is absurd. The author refutes this by analyzing the modal status of the minor premise ('illness is in every human'). If the minor premise is necessary (i.e., illness is necessarily in every human), then the conclusion that health is in no human would be true. But if the minor premise is only possibly true or absolutely true (without necessity), then the conclusion must be correspondingly possible or absolute. A further error is substituting a state for the subject: for example, using 'health' (an abstract state) instead of 'healthy' (a concrete entity), potentially rather than actually. These confusions arise from mishandling quantifiers (hasr), omissions (ihmal), and relations (rabt). This passage teaches that one must carefully consider the modality and the precise terms to avoid fallacious conclusions.

Key Terms
روابط الحدود
الصغرى
ضرورية
مطلقة
الحصر
الإهمال

D) Under no condition.

Key Takeaway
The truth of a syllogistic conclusion is conditioned by the modality of its premises; ignoring modality leads to fallacious reasoning.

Errors from Improper Quantification in Syllogisms
Author illustrates errors from improper quantification in syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a syllogism's major premise is improperly quantified and evaluate the resulting illusion of truth.

Core Idea
Quantification errors create an illusion of truth: a premise that seems true when indefinite becomes false when universal.

In logic, the quantity of a premise determines its truth conditions. The author warns that when the major premise is taken as indefinite (muhmala), it may appear true, but when made universal it is false. This often arises when the middle term is not taken universally. In Islamic legal reasoning, such errors can lead to invalid rulings. This lesson will help you recognise when a premise's quantification is deceptive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
Locate the middle term in the syllogism and check if it is a universal term. The author emphasises it must not be individual (shakhsi).
2
Check the quantification of the major premise
Determine whether the major premise is universally quantified, indefinite, or particular. If it is indefinite, consider what it would look like as a universal.
3
Test truth under universal quantification
If making the major premise universal renders it false, then the indefinite form only gives an illusion of truth. The conclusion based on that premise is not reliable.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider a fiqhi syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; this drink is an intoxicant; therefore it is forbidden.' If the major premise is indefinite (e.g., not all intoxicants are forbidden in all schools), the conclusion may be suspect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون ذلك الطائر بعينه وإن كانت الشمعة تلك الشمعة. ولا يبعد أن يكون الشيء يغاير ذاته في أحواله والمغايرة لا يصح مع وحدة بالعدد. ثمَّ الأوسط إذا يعني ليس بشخصي فمن حق الكبرى أن تكون صادقة عند الحصر الكلي فيكون قولنا زيد المعني يجوز أن يدخله كل فكأنه يقول إن كل شيء يوصف بأنه زيد المعني. وبالجملة إذا أخذت الكبرى مهملة أوهمت الصدق، وإذا جعلت كلية كذبت؛ فهذا المثال وما قبله من جملة ما تكون الحدود فيه مرتبة ترتيبها ولكن في تحصيل كميتها تخليط؛ وربَّما كان التخليط في روابط الحدود. وذلك لأنه ربَّما يعبر عن القياس بأن ابتدئ من المحمولات حينئذ إلى زيادة لفظ ليس داخلا في الحدود بل هو رابطة أو نسبة رابطة ؛ ثمَّ يقع فيه اشتراك كقول من يقول إن النتيجة لا في شيء من المرض، والمرض في كل إنسان قبل، وقد يظن أنه يعرض من هذا أن الصحة غير ممكنة أن تكون في واحد من الناس. ثم لقائل أن يقول: إنه إحدى (١) النتيجة ضرورية ولا يلزم أن يكون كذلك وإن كان قياسًا. والذي يظن أن يلزم عنه هو أن الصحة ليست ولا في واحد من الناس فإنه يكون حقا إذا كانت الصغرى وهي قولنا إن المرض في كل إنسان ضرورية. فأما إذا كانت ممكنة خاصة فإنه يجب أن يكون ممكنا، وكذلك إذا كانت الصغرى مطلقة وصح إطلاقها فإنه يجب أن يكون مطلقا . ثم الحالة إذا أخذت مكان ما يقبلها كما إذا أخذت الصحة مكان الصحيح والمرض مكان المريض في القوة وإن لم يكن بالفعل، فهذه أنحاء من الغلط يعرض بسبب الأمور الداخلة على الحدود مثل الحصر والإهمال والربط.
That bird is the very same even if the candle is that candle. And it is not far that a thing differs from itself in its states, and difference is not valid with numerical unity. Then the middle term, when it is not individual, it is appropriate for the major premise to be true under universal quantification, so our saying 'Zaid the meaning' allows that every may enter it, as if he says that everything described as Zaid the meaning. In summary, when the major premise is taken as indefinite, it gives the illusion of truth, and when made universal it is false; this example and the one before are among those where the limits are arranged in their order, but in obtaining their quantity there is mixing; and sometimes the mixing is in the connectives of the limits. That is because sometimes a syllogism is expressed by starting from predicates then adding a word not internal to the limits but is a connective or a relative relation; then ambiguity occurs, as when one says 'the conclusion is not in any disease, and disease is in every human before', and one might think that from this results that health is impossible to be in any person. Then a speaker may say: it is one of the necessary results and it does not necessarily follow, even if it is a syllogism. What one thinks follows is that health is not in any person, and that is true if the minor premise, which is our saying 'disease is in every human', is necessary. But if it is contingent, then it must be contingent, and likewise if the minor premise is absolute and its absoluteness is sound, then it must be absolute. Then the state when something is taken in place of what accepts it, as when health is taken in place of the healthy and disease in place of the patient in potency even if not in actuality; these are kinds of error that occur due to things that enter upon the limits, such as quantification, neglect, and connection.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage critiques common errors in syllogisms arising from improper handling of quantification and connectives. The author begins by noting a strange example about a bird and a candle to illustrate that identity and difference are not merely numerical. He then focuses on the middle term: if the middle term is not taken universally (as a universal term), the major premise must be universally quantified for validity, but then a claim like 'every Zaid' becomes problematic. If the major premise is left indefinite, it seems true; if universal, it becomes false. The author attributes this confusion to mixing in the quantity of the limits or in their connectives. He gives an explicit erroneous syllogism: 'the conclusion is not in any disease, and disease is in every human'—one might think this proves health impossible for anyone. However, the author clarifies that the conclusion depends on the modality of the minor premise: if the minor is necessary, the conclusion is necessary; if contingent, the conclusion is contingent; if absolute, absolute. Thus the error lies in ignoring modal distinctions. He also warns against substituting terms (e.g., 'health' for 'healthy') without considering potency and actuality. In fiqh reasoning, this parallels errors when using a universal rule without checking the modality of the subject, e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden, and this drink is an intoxicant'—if the minor premise is not universally necessary, the conclusion may be invalid.

Key Terms
الأوسط
الحصر الكلي
الروابط

Key Takeaway
